<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="krchn-palliative-care-1">Krchn Palliative Care 1<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#krchn-palliative-care-1" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Community Health Nursing (Kenya Medical Training College)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251229-a97f6875.jpeg" alt="img-0.jpeg"></p>

<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>UNIT: Palliative care Nursing and Oncology nursing</p>
<p>CLASS: KRCHN, March, 2019, YR. 2, SEM. 1</p>
<p>LECTURER: Njeri Karienye</p>
<p>Objectives</p>
<p>By the end of this unit the learner should be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Define terms</li>
<li>Describe the concept of palliative care</li>
<li>Explain the interdisciplinary team approach in palliative care</li>
<li>Describe holistic management of palliative care patients</li>
<li>Describe the common emergencies in palliative care patients</li>
<li>Describe bereavement process, grief process</li>
<li>Describe the self-care of the care-givers</li>
<li>Discuss the ethical-legal issues in palliative care</li>
</ul>
<p>Introduction</p>
<ul>
<li>Kenya Hospices and Palliative Care Association (KEHPCA) in 2006 carried out a needs assessment survey in the centers providing palliative care and found that there is definite limited skill resource in terms of numbers of service providers and even the level of skill.</li>
<li>There was also a lack of awareness on E.g HIV&amp;AIDS Palliative care and inadequate training of both health care providers and non-health care providers in HIV&amp;AIDS palliative care.</li>
<li>This has resulted in the unmet need for palliative care for those who are already affected and infected by HIV&amp;AIDS, cancer and other life threatening diseases.</li>
<li>The number of service providers was also found to be low with limited resources to support Palliative Care Services.</li>
<li>The report recommended training on Palliative care</li>
</ul>
<p>Definition</p>
<ul>
<li>Palliative care is an approach that improves the quality of life of patients and their families facing the problems associated with life-threatening illness, through the prevention and relief of suffering by means of early identification and impeccable assessment and treatment of pain and other problems, E.g physical, psychosocial and spiritual (WHO, 2002)</li>
</ul>
<p>Palliative care is part of the essential package of care for all people living with life limiting illnesses. In sub-Saharan Africa it has been developed with a specific remit for people living with HIV/AIDS and cancer, but is also needed for those with other chronic life-limiting illnesses such as end-stage organ failure.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="aim-of-palliative-care">Aim of palliative care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aim-of-palliative-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Achieving the best possible quality of life for all patients and their families by meeting their physical, psychological and spiritual needs</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="about-palliative-care-importance-of-palliative-care">About palliative care / importance of palliative care:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#about-palliative-care-importance-of-palliative-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Provides relief from pain and other distressing symptoms</li>
<li>Affirms life and regards dying as a normal process</li>
<li>Intends neither to hasten nor postpone death</li>
<li>Integrates the psychological and spiritual aspects of patient care</li>
<li>Offers a support system to help patients live as actively as possible until death</li>
<li>Offers a support system to help the family cope during the patients illness and in their own bereavement</li>
<li>Uses a team approach to address the needs of patients and their families, including bereavement counselling, if indicated</li>
<li>Will enhance quality of life and may also positively influence the course of illness</li>
<li>Is applicable early in the course of illness, in conjunction with other therapies that are intended to prolong life, such as chemotherapy or radiation therapy, and includes those investigations needed to better understand and manage distressing clinical complications.</li>
<li>Palliative care employs a holistic approach, which enables people to die with dignity while supporting those close to them.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="main-barriers-to-palliative-care-development">Main barriers to palliative care development<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#main-barriers-to-palliative-care-development" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Psychological barriers</li>
<li>Social barriers</li>
<li>Cultural barriers</li>
<li>Financial barriers</li>
</ul>
<p>The development of palliative care has been limited by additional human factors. Both hospice and palliative care have come to be associated with the end of life. Psychologically most people fear and avoid anything relating to death. There is often a belief that even acknowledging the possibility that one may die soon is harmful. There is no evidence to support this belief and recent evidence points to the possibility that palliative care may actually improve survival.</p>
<p>However, efforts to engage the public and policy makers on the need for palliative care have met with limited success. In many countries, informing patients of their diagnosis and prognosis when he/she has life threatening illness, is prohibited or strongly discouraged. Previously this had been the case in most cultures, but views have been changing in the past several decades to the point where there is now some acceptance of the idea that</p>
<p>patients themselves should be given the choice as to whether to receive this information. Life-threatening illness can have a major impact financially. In many countries effective medical treatment may only be available to those with the financial resources to pay for care.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="principles-of-palliative-care">Principles of palliative care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#principles-of-palliative-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Palliative care is based on the philosophy of total care and it employs the following principles:</p>
<p>a) Teamwork</p>
<ul>
<li>Involving team of medical workers, i.e. physicians, nurses, social workers, pastoral care professionals, physical and occupational therapist, pharmacist whose skills are required.</li>
</ul>
<p>b) Pain relief</p>
<ul>
<li>Ensuring that the client experience death free of avoidable pain and suffering and in accordance with the client's and family's issue</li>
</ul>
<p>c) Support</p>
<ul>
<li>Ensuring availability of support for the client to enable them carry out activities they enjoy doing that improve the quality of their life.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="world-health-organization-who-public-health-model-of-palliative-care">World Health Organization (WHO) Public Health Model of palliative care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#world-health-organization-who-public-health-model-of-palliative-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>This model has 4 key pillars namely:</li>
<li>Physical: e.g Nursing, treating, prescribing treatment</li>
<li>Psychological: e.g Listening, counseling, being there for them</li>
<li>Social: e.g Help with finances, housing, family support</li>
<li>Spiritual: e.g Prayer, counseling, carrying out rituals or rites.</li>
</ul>
<p>To effectively integrate palliative care into a society and change the experience of patients and families, all the four pillars of the WHO Public Health Model must be addressed. Therefore to address the pillars, the following components of palliative care must be there/present according to the same model:</p>
<ol>
<li>Appropriate policies</li>
</ol>
<p>Start by identifying key policy stakeholders. These frequently include both national and regional/county government policy makers and regulators. Review national health policies and related regulations to establish whether they incorporate the relief of pain and palliative care as priorities. If they do not, advocate for incorporation of both of these concepts as priorities into the National Health Plan, legislation, and regulations as they evolve. Review existing funding and service delivery models for providing care within the country. Determine how they might support the delivery of palliative care both at the community level and within existing acute and long-term care organizations through home care and consult services, and palliative care units. Review how doctors and other health care professionals providing palliative care are reimbursed.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Establish if the country has an essential medicines policy. If they do, include opioids and other palliative care medicines in the policy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-adequate-drug-availability">2) Adequate drug availability<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-adequate-drug-availability" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Identify leaders and start by identifying national opinion leaders responsible for drug availability, e.g., drug regulators, pharmacologists, pharmacists and law enforcement officials. Ensure that they are all engaged in the process and willing to review and improve drug availability policies and practices. Estimate Opioid Need. Use a simple strategy to estimate the annual opioid need for E.g the cancer patients. Assume that 60% of patients with advanced cancer will be the principal consumers and that they will each use an average of 100 mg morphine per day for the last 100 days (three months) of their lives, i.e., a total of 10 g of morphine per patient. This technique will estimate only a portion of the total opioid need for the country. As an example, for a country with five million people and 3,500 new diagnoses of cancer per year, two thirds of which present with stage III or IV cancer, if 60% of these patients (2,100) have pain and need 10 g of morphine each before they die, that country will need 21 kg of morphine per year just to manage these cancer patients' pain. Ensure an Affordable Supply.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-education-of-health-care-workers-and-the-public">3) Education of health care workers and the public<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-education-of-health-care-workers-and-the-public" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>i) Identify leaders: Start by identifying national opinion leaders responsible for education e.g., clinical and education experts, deans of medical schools, nursing, pharmacy and social work schools. Ensure that they are all engaged in the process and willing to change existing educational curricula and courses and develop new ones.</p>
<p>ii) Identify target audiences: To implement palliative care effectively across the country, there will be many different target audiences who will require education to increase their awareness and change their attitudes, knowledge and skills related to palliative care. These include: the media and public, medical, nursing, social work/ psychology and pharmacy health care professionals and trainees, experts training in palliative care, spiritual leaders and patients and families.</p>
<p>iii) Media and public advocacy: Develop an educational intervention and advocacy tools to engage the media and heighten public awareness of the need for and benefits of palliative care for patients at any time during an illness experience.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-implementation-of-palliative-care-services-at-all-levels-throughout-the-society">4) Implementation of palliative care services at all levels throughout the society.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-implementation-of-palliative-care-services-at-all-levels-throughout-the-society" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Palliative care programme development has been very uneven internationally and is correlated with Human Development Index levels. Without policy support for palliative care and funding mechanisms, growth is restricted to whatever is achieved by pioneers making sacrifices to create hospice and palliative care services in their communities</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="approaches-of-palliative-care">Approaches of palliative care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#approaches-of-palliative-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-patient-centred">i) Patient centred<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-patient-centred" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>In order to improve the quality of life, a patient centred care is vital.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>It helps in defining wishes and goals</li>
<li>Also assist the patient to obtain the type of care they want</li>
</ul>
<p>ii) Support for the caregiver</p>
<ul>
<li>The role of a caregiver is a vital one and there are many resources to assist</li>
<li>Enhance the caregiver’s skills with the right information</li>
</ul>
<p>Essential elements of quality palliative homecare:</p>
<ul>
<li>Integrated teamwork</li>
<li>Management of pain and physical symptoms</li>
<li>Holistic care</li>
<li>Caring, compassionate and skilled providers</li>
<li>Timely and responsive care</li>
<li>Patient and family preparedness</li>
</ul>
<p>Essential components of palliative care</p>
<ul>
<li>Pain and symptom control</li>
<li>Effective communication</li>
<li>Rehabilitation</li>
<li>Continuity of care</li>
<li>Breaking of bad news</li>
<li>Psychosocial care</li>
<li>Spiritual care</li>
<li>Social care</li>
<li>Terminal/ end of life</li>
<li>Education</li>
<li>Research</li>
</ul>
<p>NB: Ability to support and to meet the needs of dying clients and their support systems is affected by a multitude of factors.</p>
<p>These factors include:</p>
<ul>
<li>The initial stage of the time of diagnosis</li>
<li>Post and present treatments</li>
<li>Whether the client is experiencing a recurrence or progression of the disease</li>
<li>The client’s relationship with the health care provider</li>
<li>The health care provider experience and comfort level with death and dying issues</li>
<li>The client’s family/support system or lack thereof</li>
<li>The symptoms he/she is experiencing</li>
<li>His/her own fears and concerns about death</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="physiological-changes-towards-the-end-of-life">Physiological changes towards The End of Life<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#physiological-changes-towards-the-end-of-life" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Decreased urine output, resulting in the need for the urinal, bedpan, commode, and /or characterization.</li>
<li>Decreased GI fluid, resulting in decreased episodes of vomiting, need for NGT.</li>
<li>Decreased pulmonary secretions, resulting in decreased cough, coughing, chocking and suctioning</li>
<li>Decreased pharyngeal secretions for those too weak to swallow, less likely to feel like drowning.</li>
<li>Decrease fluid overload resulting in decreased peripheral and pulmonary edema</li>
<li>Reabsorption of fluid, resulting in decreased ascites decreased peri-tumor edema with decreased pain.</li>
<li>Decreased wound and fistula drainage</li>
<li>Decreased intracranial pressure in some patients resulting in increased lucidity</li>
<li>Decreased care, cost and burden of maintain hydration</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management-of-signs-and-symptoms-for-common-problems-in-the-terminally-ill-patients">Management of signs and symptoms (for common problems in the terminally ill patients)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-signs-and-symptoms-for-common-problems-in-the-terminally-ill-patients" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The common problems in the terminally ill patients are:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="a-pain">A) Pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>These patients are usually in very severe pain. They are therefore put on medications that help alleviate pain/ reduce it to a bearable level.</li>
<li>This is aimed at ensuring that the client experience death free of avoidable pain and suffering and in accordance with the client's and family's issue.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pain-management">Pain management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pain-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Relieve pain as fast as possible and prevent its return.</li>
<li>Use pharmacologic and/or non-pharmacologic methods</li>
<li>Control pain while treating the underlying cause (e.g. infection)</li>
<li>Reassess pain regularly using assessment tools</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="non-pharmacological-pain-management">Non-pharmacological pain management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#non-pharmacological-pain-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This can be:</p>
<p><strong>Physical</strong>: E.g. massage, exercise, physiotherapy, surgery</p>
<p><strong>Psychological</strong>: Strengthen the patient's coping mechanisms through counselling, relaxation therapies etc.</p>
<p><strong>Social</strong>: Help the patient resolve social or cultural problems through community resources, financial and legal support, etc.</p>
<p><strong>Spiritual</strong>: E.g. religious counselling and prayer.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pharmacological-pain-management">Pharmacological pain management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pharmacological-pain-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Use WHO guidelines i.e</p>
<p>Pain management should be by the:</p>
<ul>
<li>Mouth: Use the oral route where possible.</li>
<li>Clock: Analgesia should be given at fixed time intervals.
Give the next dose before pain recurs.</li>
<li>Individual: Involve adults and children fully in their care.
Link doses to their daily routine</li>
<li>Ladder: Choose analgesics according to the World Health Organization (WHO) analgesic ladder covering mild, moderate and severe pain.</li>
<li>The choice of analgesic depends on the severity, site and type of pain.</li>
<li>Start patients on Step 1 analgesics for mild pain, if ineffective then, Change to a Step 2 analgesic</li>
<li>Then to Step 3 as required, If Step 1 or 2 analgesics don't work</li>
<li>Don't switch to another analgesic at the same level, move up a step</li>
<li>A combination of a non-opioid and an opioid drug is effective (they have different modes of action).</li>
<li>Don't combine weak with strong opioids.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="other-medications-for-managing-pain-adjuvants">Other medications for managing pain - (adjuvants)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#other-medications-for-managing-pain-adjuvants" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Adjuvants can be combined with Step 1, 2 or 3 drugs</li>
<li>If the oral route isn't possible, use alternative methods.</li>
<li>These can include rectal, intravenous, nasogastric tube, transdermal and subcutaneous routes.</li>
<li>The majority of patients can have their pain controlled in the home care/outpatient settings using the WHO analgesic ladder as a guide.</li>
<li>Only in very severe cases may they need to be an inpatient.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6d853583">Mild pain – Step 1<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6d853583" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="paracetamol">Paracetamol<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#paracetamol" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Adult dose: 500mg–1g p.o 6hrly; maximum daily dose 4g.</li>
<li>NB: Hepatotoxicity can occur if more than maximum dose is given per day.</li>
<li>Can be combined with a non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drug (NSAID)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="children">Children<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#children" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>under 1 year 10–15mg/kg p.o 6–8hrly;</li>
<li>1–5 years 10–15mg/kg p.o 6–8hrly</li>
<li>5–12 years 250–500mg p.o 6–8hrly.</li>
<li>Maximum dose 75mg/kg/day.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ibuprofen-nsaid">Ibuprofen (NSAID)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ibuprofen-nsaid" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Adult dose: 400mg p.o 6–8hrly. Maximum dose 1.2g per day.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Caution: can cause serious side effects, e.g. gastro-intestinal (GI) bleeding or renal toxicity.</li>
<li>If GI symptoms occur, stop and give H2 reception antagonist (e.g. Ranitidine).</li>
</ul>
<p>In children:</p>
<p><strong>5mg/kg po 6–8hrly.</strong></p>
<p><strong>Maximum 30mg/kg/day in three or four divided doses</strong></p>
<p><strong>Diclofenac (NSAID)</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Adult dose: 50mg po 8hrly. Maximum dose 150mg per day</li>
</ul>
<p>In children</p>
<ul>
<li>6 months to 12 years: 2–3mg/kg per 24hrs po in two or three doses</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Moderate pain – Step 2 (weak opioids)</strong></p>
<p><strong>Codeine</strong></p>
<p>Codeine is the commonest weak opioid:</p>
<ul>
<li>Adult dose: 30–60mg po 4 hrly (maximum dose 180–240mg per day),</li>
<li>Codeine is often combined with Step 1 analgesics.</li>
<li>Give laxatives to avoid constipation unless patient has diarrhoea.</li>
<li>If pain relief is not achieved on the ceiling dose (max dose 240mg per day), move to a strong opioid</li>
</ul>
<p>Children</p>
<p>Over 6 months: 0.5–1mg/kg p.o 6hrly</p>
<p><strong>Tramadol</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Adult dose: 50–100mg p.o 4–6hrly.</li>
<li>NB: Start with a regular small dose and increase if no response is observed.</li>
<li>Dose limit is 400mg/day.</li>
</ul>
<p>Children</p>
<p>Over 12 years: 50–100mg p.o 4–6hrly</p>
<p><strong>Severe pain – Step 3 (strong opioids)</strong></p>
<p><strong>Morphine</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Opioid-naive infants &lt;6 months, starting dose 0.02mg/kg p.o 4hrly;</li>
<li>Opioid-naive infants &gt;6 months, starting dose 0.04 mg/kg p.o 4hrly.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="children">Children<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#children" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Younger than six months are more sensitive to possible opioid-induced respiratory depression, so they need a lower starting dose</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="morphine">Morphine<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Morphine is the 'gold standard' against which other analgesics are measured.</li>
<li>The correct morphine dose is the one that gives pain relief, there is no 'ceiling' or maximum dose, but any increase should be gradual.</li>
<li>Starting dose is 2.5–20mg p.o 4hrly, the level depending on age, previous use of opiates, etc.</li>
<li>Patients changing from regular administration of a Step 2 opioid should start on morphine 10 mg p.o 4hrly.</li>
<li>If patient is cachexic, or not on Step 2 analgesics, start morphine at 5mg p.o 4hrly.</li>
<li>Start frail/elderly patients on morphine at 2.5mg .po 6–8hrly, due to the likelihood of impaired renal function. Breakthrough' or 'rescue' doses of morphine can be given as often as required (ideally the same as the 4hrly dose).</li>
<li>Keep a record of each rescue dose.</li>
<li>Titrate the regular dose of morphine over several days until the patient is pain free.</li>
<li>Either add the total daily dose and the total breakthrough dose given in 24 hours and divide by six to get a new 4hrly dose, or give 30–50% increments (e.g. 5–10–15mg etc.) as 4hrly doses.</li>
<li>Increments of less than 30% are ineffective. If needed, give a double dose of morphine at night to allow pain-free sleep.</li>
<li>If the patient can't swallow, use other routes, e.g. rectal, subcutaneous, buccal, intravenous or gastrostomy tube.</li>
<li>Ratio of morphine P.O: SC is 2:1, e.g. 10mg of oral morphine equates to 5mg of SC morphine.</li>
<li>Ratio of morphine: P.O: IV is 2–3:1, e.g. 30mg of oral morphine equates to 10mg of IV morphine.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="morphine-side-effects-and-management">Morphine side effects and management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphine-side-effects-and-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The health care provider ought to explain common morphine side-effects to patients and prevent where possible:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="constipation">Constipation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#constipation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Always give with a laxative, e.g. bisacodyl 5mg at night increasing to 15 mg if needed.</li>
<li>Do not give laxative if the patient has diarrhoea.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nausea-and-vomiting">Nausea and vomiting<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nausea-and-vomiting" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Give metoclopramide 10mg 8hrly, or haloperidol 1.5 mg once a day.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="drowsiness">Drowsiness<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#drowsiness" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>May occur in the first few days, if it does not improve after about three days, cut down on morphine dose.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="itching">Itching<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#itching" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Less common but if it occurs, give chlorpheniramine.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nb">NB:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nb" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Patients on a stable morphine dose should not be sedated.</li>
<li>If sedated, reduce the dose and consider adjuvants.</li>
<li>Morphine is available in immediate-release and slow-release oral formulations.</li>
<li>Use slow-release morphine once pain is controlled.</li>
<li>Divide the total 24-hour dose into two to get the twice-daily dosage.</li>
<li>Fentanyl patches can be started once pain is under control on morphine and you know the amount of analgesia the patient needs in 24 hours.</li>
<li>Don't use Fentanyl for acute pain.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="adjuvant-analgesics">Adjuvant analgesics<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#adjuvant-analgesics" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Although their primary purpose is not analgesic, these medications relieve pain through other mechanisms</li>
<li>Adjuvants are particularly useful in pain that is only partially sensitive to opioids, e.g. neuropathic and bone pain, smooth or skeletal muscle spasms, or pain related to anxiety.</li>
<li>Use adjuvants alone or in conjunction with Step 1, 2 and 3 analgesics.</li>
</ul>
<p>Examples of the types of adjuvants that are relevant to palliative care are :</p>
<p>Antidepressants, Anticonvulsants, Antispasmodics, Muscle relaxants / Anxiolytics, Corticosteroids</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="antidepressants">Antidepressants<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#antidepressants" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Use for neuropathic pain, presenting primarily as burning or dysaesthesia. Eg: Amitriptyline.</li>
<li>Adult dose 10–75 mg at night, where you should start with a low dose and slowly increase it as needed.</li>
<li>Can also be given in a dose of 0.5–2 mg/kg at night.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="side-effects-include">Side effects include:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#side-effects-include" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Dry mouth</li>
<li>Drowsiness.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nb-use-with-caution-in-the-elderly-and-those-with-cardiac-disease">NB: Use with caution in the elderly and those with cardiac disease.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nb-use-with-caution-in-the-elderly-and-those-with-cardiac-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="anticonvulsants">Anticonvulsants<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#anticonvulsants" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Use for neuropathic pain. Eg: Carbamazepine.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Adult dose should start at 100mg twice a day, and can be increased to 800mg twice a day.</li>
<li>Sodium valporate 200mg – 1.2g per day.</li>
<li>Gabapentin 100mg three times a day, increase up to 3.6g daily.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>NB:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Use Phenytoin in the absence of these drugs, at the rate of 100mg 2–3 times/day</li>
<li>Use Phenytoin and Carbamazepine with caution because of the rapid metabolism of other drugs metabolised in the liver.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Antispasmodics</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Use for muscle spasm, such as colicky abdominal pain or renal colic. Eg Hyoscine Butylbromide (Buscopan)</li>
<li>Dose: Adult: start at 10mg three times /day, Can be increased to 40mg three times/day</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>NB.</strong>
Can cause nausea, dry mouth and constipation.</p>
<p><strong>Muscle relaxants / Anxiolytics</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Use for skeletal muscle spasm and anxiety-related pain. Eg Diazepam.</li>
<li>Adult dose 5mg orally 2–3 times/day</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Corticosteriods</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Use for bone pain, neuropathic pain, headache due to raised intracranial pressure, or pain associated with oedema and inflammation. Eg: Dexamethasone.</li>
<li>Adult dose 2–4mg per day for most situations, apart from raised intracranial pressure, nerve compression and spinal cord compression.</li>
<li>For raised Intracranial pressure start at 24mg per day and reduce by 2mg daily to the lowest effective maintenance dose.</li>
<li>For nerve-compression types of pain 8mg is often used and for spinal cord compression 16mg is usually the starting dose.</li>
</ul>
<p>If dexamethasone is not available, then adults can also be given prednisolone where a conversion rate of 4mg dexamethasone to 30mg prednisolone can be used.</p>
<p><strong>NB:</strong> In advanced disease, corticosteroids may improve appetite, decrease nausea and malaise and improve quality of life.</p>
<p><strong>Adverse effects include</strong>
Neuropsychiatric syndromes, gastrointestinal disturbances and immunosuppression</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="recommendations-on-pain-management-using-drugs-by-world-health-organization-who">Recommendations on Pain management using drugs by World Health Organization (WHO)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recommendations-on-pain-management-using-drugs-by-world-health-organization-who" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The drugs should be given:</p>
<p>i) By Mouth</p>
<p>This should be the route of choice because it is convenient and easy. If no longer tolerable, subcutaneous route should be used.</p>
<p>ii) By Clock</p>
<p>This means that drugs should be given regularly at the same time.</p>
<p>iii) By ladder</p>
<p>This means that you start with one drug that is the weakest, increase the dosage as necessary and only move to the next stronger drug after exhausting the potential of the weaker drug e.g. you cannot move straight to opioid drugs such as morphine before you finish exploiting paracetamol.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-who-analgesic-ladder">The WHO Analgesic Ladder:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-who-analgesic-ladder" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Step 1: Non Opioids: -</p>
<p>These are drugs such as Paracetamol and other non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs.</p>
<p>Step 2: Mild Opioids: -</p>
<p>These are drugs such as Codeine often combined with Paracetamol.</p>
<p>Step 3: Strong Opioids: -</p>
<p>In this category, there is morphine which can be of immediate release e.g. Oramorph and of sustained release e.g. subcutaneous Diamorphine</p>
<p>B) Anorexia</p>
<p>These patients are unable to eat because of being weak, anxious, depressed and nauseated</p>
<p>The patient should therefore:</p>
<ul>
<li>Be encouraged to feed</li>
<li>Given drugs that reduce nausea</li>
<li>Given the opportunity to choose diet</li>
<li>Given cool and more appealing drinks</li>
<li>In case patient can't feed orally, alternative methods should be used</li>
</ul>
<p>C) Nausea and Vomiting</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>This can be reduced by giving treatment for the same</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Patient should be positioned properly to prevent aspiration.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Anti - emetics should be administered, dry foods and carbonated beverages should be encouraged as they are less nauseating</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="d-depression">D) Depression<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#d-depression" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>It requires treatment and referral to psychiatrists for management</li>
<li>Social support from family and friends is also essential so that the patient does not feel neglected</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="e-diarrhea">E) Diarrhea<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#e-diarrhea" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>It may results from side effects of medication, infection and change in diet</li>
<li>Patients should be given low residue diet</li>
<li>Gas causing foods should be eliminated from the diet</li>
<li>Patient should be encouraged to take fluids other than plain water to replace lost electrolytes</li>
<li>Perineal care essential</li>
<li>In case of constipation, high residue diet should be given and nurse should continuously monitor</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="f-dehydration">F) Dehydration<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#f-dehydration" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Results from reduced intake of fluids</li>
<li>NB: Some dehydration is beneficial as it dries secretions and reduces the risk of fluid accumulation in cavities but severe dehydration should not be allowed</li>
<li>Oral fluids should be administered and if need be IV fluids should be used.</li>
<li>Monitor input- output accurately</li>
<li>Mouth care should be maintained</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="g-skin-breakdown">G) Skin breakdown<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#g-skin-breakdown" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Most patients are bed ridden because they are weak or depressed</li>
<li>Adequate nutrition and hydration should be maintained to promote skin integrity</li>
<li>Pressure area care should be done</li>
<li>Position changing should be done frequently (at least 2 hourly) to prevent bed sore</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="h-respiratory-problems">H) Respiratory problems<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#h-respiratory-problems" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>They are common due to retained secretions which result from inability to expectorate and immobility</li>
<li>Respiration rate and depth should be monitored</li>
<li>Chest physiotherapy should also be done</li>
<li>Oxygen should be administered when necessary</li>
<li>Suctioning should be done in case of increased secretions</li>
<li>Appropriate positioning necessary</li>
<li>Drugs that reduce secretions may be used e.g. atropine</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="domains-of-palliative-care">Domains of palliative care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#domains-of-palliative-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-disease-management">a) Disease management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-disease-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Primary diagnosis</li>
<li>Secondary diagnoses or co-morbidities</li>

<li>Rites, rituals</li>
<li>Pronouncement, certification of death</li>
<li>Care of family at time of and following death eg. privacy, involvement in care.</li>
<li>Arrangements for the body eg. mortuary viewing</li>
<li>Funerals, memorial services</li>
</ul>

<p>Model of care</p>
<p>A Palliative Care Model is proposed to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Provide services for patients with life limiting illness, regardless of diagnosis</li>
<li>Address the palliative care needs of patients and their families during their illness trajectory</li>
<li>Deliver care in any setting - hospital, palliative care unit, residential care or home</li>
<li>Identify partnerships between specialist palliative care services and primary care providers.</li>
</ul>
<p>Principles of the model</p>
<p>The principles that underpin this Palliative Care Model are:</p>
<ul>
<li>A life-limiting illness is an illness that can be reasonably expected to cause the death of a patient within a foreseeable future.</li>
<li>The patient and family is the unit that should understand that, the person’s illness affects not only the person who is unwell but also the family and any difficulties experienced by the family will in turn have impact on the person who is unwell.</li>
<li>Palliative care is multidisciplinary and is an integral part of the health care system. Every person with a life-limiting illness has a fundamental right to access care that is underpinned by a palliative approach.</li>
<li>There is a formal process of assessment, informed consent inclusive of boundaries of care and decision making around advance care plans. For example, orders for “not for resuscitation”.</li>
<li>Quality of life can be enhanced by access to physical, psychological, emotional, social and spiritual support provided in a culturally appropriate manner.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>People with a life-limiting illness and their families, will have access to information about options for future care so that they are able to actively participate in decision making about treatment, care and end-of-life issues.</li>
<li>People with life limiting illness will have access to quality care to meet their needs. This care is delivered by trained staffs who are supported by specialist palliative care services.</li>
<li>People with life limiting illness have the right to spend as much time as possible in their own home and community, therefore out patient follow-up will be provided where possible in local community settings, or by using Telehealth and other technologies, to avoid unnecessary travel and burden to patients and families.</li>
<li>Patients and families need to experience a seamless service system, with smooth and timely transitions from one service to another. This includes:</li>
<li>Timely referral to a palliative care service (preferably not in crisis)</li>
<li>Effective communication with the patient and family and between services</li>
<li>Speedy and straightforward admission to appropriate inpatient care when required</li>
<li>Speedy and straightforward transfer from acute care to an appropriate palliative care service setting if this is required</li>
<li>Continuity of health care provision across the various care settings</li>
<li>Coordination and integration of services across all settings with other medical, health or community care providers.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="coordination-of-palliative-care">Coordination of palliative care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#coordination-of-palliative-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This can be achieved in a variety of ways and will depend on local circumstances. Each team should identify the other teams/services with which they interact most frequently and should develop plans to promote coordinated care. These plans may include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Establishing joint out-patient or community-based clinics</li>
<li>Undertaking joint ward rounds/case review</li>
<li>Participating in multidisciplinary meetings that include people from different services.</li>
</ul>
<p>The need for palliative care often goes unrecognized as there are misconceptions about the skills and expertise that can be offered by the specialist palliative care team, even as early as the diagnosis of the patient's illness. The following framework details the steps involved in the palliative care therapeutic process that guides the interactions between health professionals, patient and families.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="steps-in-palliative-care-process-process-of-providing-palliative-care">Steps in palliative care process / Process of providing palliative care /<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#steps-in-palliative-care-process-process-of-providing-palliative-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-assessment">1. Assessment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-assessment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Patient and family history including presenting problems and current, past and potential issues.</li>
<li>Physical examination</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>What social structures and supports are in place? How do the family function? – family or carers, other support structures (health facility, community), health issues for family or carers, functional/practical issues, financial issues</li>
<li>Psychological and emotional responses to the illness and its effects, what was the pre-illness personality and how did they usually manage and cope, fears, anxiety, understanding, concerns for family, previous psychological/psychiatric issues, Intelligence, cognition, needs (intervention/counselling/ medication).</li>
<li>Spirituality/religious beliefs i.e existential concerns, spiritual/religious needs, rites or rituals, religious paraphernalia, staff awareness/knowledge of these needs and where to seek help.</li>
<li>Expectations regarding the care and the outcomes from the contact</li>
<li>Are there any priorities of care for the patient or family</li>
<li>What is the complexity of the care that is, or that may be required.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-communication">2. Communication<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-communication" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The patient and family’s understanding of the illness and its actual or potential impact on both</li>
<li>What information they require?</li>
<li>How prepared they are for this information?</li>
<li>To manage how the information should be provided.</li>
<li>To determine when and where the information should be provided</li>
<li>Who information can be or should be shared with.</li>
<li>How they respond to the information provided?</li>
<li>The understanding of what has been provided.</li>
<li>Is there a need for additional information</li>
<li>Is a translator or translation of information required?</li>
<li>Has consent to share information been given?</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-importance-of-effective-communication-in-palliative-care">The importance of effective communication in palliative care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-importance-of-effective-communication-in-palliative-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Family members often recall in detail the sensitivity or lack of it, of the doctor or staff as their relative was dying. These memories affect the grieving process, how the family was told about what was being done, how they were informed of the changes in the medical situation and especially how attentive the doctor and staff were in controlling the patient’s distress and physical symptoms.</p>
<p>Effective communication fosters a very strong relationship between the caregiver and the patient as well as family members. Truthful communication about the future is also vital if patients are to be permitted the dignity of deciding how to spend their remaining time. Good communication maintains effective professional relationships, which fosters a high standard of care. Communication is seen as a therapy, which is used to help the patient to either cope with or solve the problem.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="possible-consequences-of-ineffective-communication-in-palliative-care">Possible consequences of ineffective communication in palliative care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#possible-consequences-of-ineffective-communication-in-palliative-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Not communicating accurate essential information to patients may provoke greater problems. Protecting patients from the reality of their situation often creates further problems and can lead to inconsistent messages being given by other members of the inter-disciplinary team. Hiding the truth often leads to conspiracies of silence that usually build up to a heightened state of fear, anxiety and confusion, rather than provide one of calmness. Poor communication is a threat to patient care and can lead both to mistrust and to a source of staff stress. Communicating effectively is essential for engaging the patient and their family in their care. Not communicating about the nature and seriousness of an illness can lead to a lack of planning for the future e.g. not writing a will, not planning who will take care, etc</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-decision-making">3. Decision making<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-decision-making" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>What is the capacity of the patient and family to make any decisions?</li>
<li>Have the goals of care been determined?</li>
<li>Are there any priorities for the care that is required?</li>
<li>What are the treatment options available and what are the benefits, risks and burdens associated with these?</li>
<li>What are the treatment choices?</li>
<li>Has consent for treatment been obtained?</li>
<li>Have there been any requests to withhold or withdraw therapy?</li>
<li>Have there been any requests to be given therapy that offers no benefit?</li>
<li>Requests to hasten death?</li>
<li>Have “Advance directives” been recorded?</li>
<li>If there is conflict is it possible to resolve this?</li>
<li>Legal guardianship/Surrogate decision making/Power of attorney</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-care-planning">4. Care planning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-care-planning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Negotiation of process of plan of care that includes:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Discussion about the issues</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Ways to deal with these issues.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Treatment options to manage the issues.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>What type of care is required?</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Who should provide the care?</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Where the care should be provided?</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Have the needs of both the family and/or carers been factored into the plan?</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Support(s)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Despite</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dealing with emergencies</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Discharge planning</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Terminal Care</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Bereavement</p>
</li>

<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="5">
<li>Care delivery</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Determine the composition of the team(s) required to provide the care.
&gt; Based on the level of need and complexity of the care.
&gt; Do family members have any role in the provision of care?</li>
<li>Coordination and leadership of the team(s) involved in the care.</li>
<li>Education and training requirements of the care team</li>
<li>Education and training needs of the family/carers.</li>
<li>Is any support required by the team providing the care?</li>
<li>What support is required by the family/carers?</li>
<li>Where is the care going to be provided and is this likely to change at any time due to the patients or family’s requirements?</li>
<li>What services are essential and what services may have a supportive role?</li>
<li>Practical issues related to the care e.g supply/storage of equipment, dealing with infectious wastes.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="6">
<li>Endorsement of the plan</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>How able are the patient and family to participate in the care planning?</li>
<li>What is everyone’s understanding – including health carers of what is discussed and decided upon?</li>
<li>Are there any additional questions or issues which need to be addressed</li>
<li>Is everyone satisfied with the plan of care?</li>
<li>Does the plan of care cause distress to anyone in the group?</li>
<li>How complex is the care required and what are the implications both for the patient and family and for the health care staff?</li>
</ul>
<p>Models of palliative care delivery</p>
<p>To meet the challenge of providing palliative care in Kenya, a variety of models for palliative care delivery have been developed, which include the following examples listed below. All of these models can be found at specialist, county level and primary care / community levels of the health system.</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Specialist palliative care organisations (Eg. Nairobi Hospice in Kenya, kisumu hospice.
Specialist services may be provided across the continuum of care at tertiary, secondary and primary levels of care. They also provide an element of leadership, training, mentorship and supervision.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Hospital-based palliative care teams (Eg. Kenyatta National Referral Hospital):
These teams provide specialist services within the hospital context and this is a model of palliative care delivery that is growing in the region. In Kenya, the Kenya Hospices and Palliative Care Association (KEHPCA) is working with the government to train teams in various regional hospitals and to increase access to palliative care services across the country.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Inpatient services</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>19</p>
<p>While the majority of palliative care in the region is provided in the home, or through hospital-based teams, inpatient services can provide care in particular for patients with difficult symptoms that cannot be brought under control in the home.</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p><strong>Home-based care</strong>
Much of palliative care provision in Kenya occurs in the home. It may be provided through specialist palliative care teams that visit patients and support them in the home, or else through home-based care services provided by community volunteers and supported by palliative care staff. In Africa home-based care is a well established model of care provision within the context of HIV/AIDS.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Outreach services</strong>
Some palliative care providers have outreach services that support other organisations to provide palliative care, or provide roadside and mobile clinics. These services help to increase accessibility to palliative care service provision for those in rural and difficult to reach communities.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Importance of standards in palliative care</strong></p>
</li>
<li>
<p>To provide common understanding of the work to be done and indicate a level of quality for services provision</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>To help to ensure that the quality of services being provided is maintained as operational coverage is extended</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>To help the process of continuous improvement of services quality by determining those areas of service provision where problems that require remedial attention exist</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>To serve to inform patients and their families of what quality of care they expect and should demand as services users</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>To enable staff to know what they are expected to provide</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>To serve as a resource for palliative care training</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Palliative care service require skills in the following areas:</strong></p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Communication</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Decision-making</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Management of the complication of treatment and diseases</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Management of pain and symptoms</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Psycho-social care for patient and family</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Spiritual understanding and approaches</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Care of the dying</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Bereavement care</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Network of palliative care teams across the levels of health care</strong></p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>a) <strong>Community level :</strong></p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Community leaders, traditional healers and family caregivers, who are trained to provide basic home-based care and who are supervised by primary care level nurses.</li>
</ul>
<p>b) Primary care level:</p>
<ul>
<li>Nurses trained in basic palliative care who are supervised by the district/county level and who train and supervise community volunteers and family caregivers.</li>
</ul>
<p>c) Secondary care level (district /County hospital)</p>
<ul>
<li>Specialist palliative care team: physician, nurse who supervises primary health-care clinics, part-time social worker and pharmacist.</li>
</ul>
<p>d) Tertiary care level (national or regional hospital)</p>
<ul>
