<li>Specialist palliative care team: physician, nurse, part-time social worker and pharmacist. All physicians and nurses dealing with cancer patients provide basic palliative care, supervised by the specialist team.</li>
</ul>
<p>Across the levels of care, services can be provided through the following:</p>
<p>Inpatient care</p>
<p>In low-income countries, where a stand-alone unit for inpatients would be relatively cost-intensive, a specialized palliative care team is an effective way of providing palliative care services to inpatients in a variety of health-care settings. A specialized team may be more sustainable than a free-standing unit.</p>
<p>A palliative care team is typically composed of a physician trained in palliative medicine, clinical nurses and social worker, all supported by adequate administrative staff. Such a team has no dedicated beds of its own but advises on every aspect of palliative care. This approach makes it possible to provide palliative care consultations to a large number of patients, in a hospital or nursing home. It is an ideal way of providing specialist care while at the same time training the large numbers of physicians and nurses with whom the team interacts.</p>
<p>Outpatient care</p>
<p>In some developing countries, the day-care unit or outpatient unit performs the important role of offering low-cost care to people who are not too sick to attend. It also offers an opportunity to review a patient's need for periodic procedures such as draining pleural effusions or ascites and to train the patient's family in how to provide care. Often, day-care services may focus on providing respite for relatives by ensuring that patients get medications, food, their physical, psychological and spiritual needs met.</p>
<p>Home-based care</p>
<p>In high-income countries, home-based care services are usually more resource-intensive than in low- and middle-income countries. They often operate from a specialist palliative care unit or hospice and sometimes provide round-the-clock coverage.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>In low- and middle-income countries, where patients usually prefer to die at home, home-based care is generally more acceptable and affordable than hospital care. E.g with the support of local non-governmental organizations, the Ministry of Health of Uganda has included pain relief and palliative care in a home-care package, based on a needs assessment of patients and their caregivers. Services include essential drugs for the relief of pain and other symptoms, as well as the provision of food for the patient and support for the patient's family (Logie and Harding, 2005).</p>
<p>A variety of models of home-based palliative care are currently being implemented in low-resource countries. A popular approach in low-resource settings, where the number of people needing care is high and the number of nurses and doctors available to provide that care is low, is to provide care through community caregivers or volunteers who are supervised by a nurse trained in palliative care. Ideally, any model of home care will have strong links to an inpatient facility for patients requiring more intensive palliative care for symptom control or for terminal care.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ethical-and-legal-issues-in-palliative-care">Ethical and legal issues in palliative care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ethical-and-legal-issues-in-palliative-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In recognizing the importance of human rights within the health field, the Kenyan Patients’ Rights Charter states that every patient has the right to:</p>
<ul>
<li>A healthy and safe environment</li>
<li>Participate in decision making</li>
<li>Access to health care</li>
<li>Knowledge of one’s health insurance/medical aid scheme</li>
<li>Choice of health services</li>
<li>Be treated by a normal health care provider</li>
<li>Confidentiality and privacy</li>
<li>Informed consent</li>
<li>Refuse treatment</li>
<li>Be referred for a second opinion</li>
<li>Continuity of care</li>
<li>Complain about health services</li>
</ul>
<p>Ethical and legal issues that should be addressed in a palliative care plan and programme include the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Allocation of adequate human and financial resources to palliative care</li>
<li>Integration of palliative care with curative care to ensure high-quality care</li>
<li>Societal, individual and professional obligations and responsibilities regarding the provision of care</li>
<li>Differing perceptions and attitudes in different cultural contexts concerning, E.g, quality-of-life versus length-of-life goals, enduring or relieving pain and the end of life</li>
<li>Autonomy of decision-making, and valid informed consent of the patient and family</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Legal and cultural barriers to accessing palliative care, in particular opioids.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Autonomy (Respecting patient’s wishes)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Help the patient to have choice and control over their life and what happens to them</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Help them to set achievable goals</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Give accurate and truthful information to the patient</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Respect confidentiality</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Get informed consent for changes in treatment</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Minimize harm</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Avoid treatments/drugs that may harm or have no overall benefit for the patient</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Stop treatments/drugs that are no longer helpful</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Be aware of the social and financial cost of treatments</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Avoid interventions that stop patients achieving their goals</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Do Good (Beneficence)</p>
<ul>
<li>Communicate well</li>
<li>Give care and treatment that will give overall benefit to the patient</li>
<li>Support the patient</li>
<li>Control pain and other symptoms</li>
</ul>
<p>Justice</p>
<ul>
<li>Respect patients’ human rights</li>
<li>Balance the needs of the individual with those of the family and society</li>
<li>Use available resources fairly</li>
</ul>
<p>The Ethical framework for palliative care</p>
<p>Autonomy</p>
<ul>
<li>Autonomy is about the respect for a person’s right to make decisions concerning their care. Autonomy promotes the development of a trusting relationship between a health care worker and a patient.</li>
<li>It also means the patient becomes an active member of the management team, which restores a sense of control in the face of an illness that has deprived that person of control.</li>
<li>In order to give due attention to this principle, several matters are involved, and these are set out next.</li>
</ul>
<p>Communication
The patient has a right to know about the diagnosis, the treatment that is proposed, its effects and any side effects.</p>
<ul>
<li>It is the responsibility of the health care worker to ensure that the patient is fully informed.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>This will involve an assessment by the health care worker both of the patient’s understanding and of how much the patient wants to know.</li>
<li>Failure to communicate honestly with the patient can isolate the patient, preventing a therapeutic sharing of the patient’s fears, anxieties and other concerns.</li>
<li>In the case of a child patient, especially an older child, opinions and concerns are often not sought. This increases the child’s pain.</li>
<li>The child can be left to feel responsible for their illness and even for their parents’ suffering.</li>
<li>It is most important for the health professional to include the child in communications with the family about the illness and in decision-making.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="consent">Consent<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#consent" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Once the patient is given sufficient information, they have the prerogative to accept or not accept the care or treatment offered.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="confidentiality">Confidentiality<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#confidentiality" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Confidentiality protects the autonomy of the patient by allowing them to control information about themselves.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="privacy">Privacy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#privacy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Central to a respect for personal autonomy is the concept that the privacy of the individual must be respected.</li>
<li>The principle of non-maleficence is not absolute, and must be balanced against</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="beneficence">Beneficence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#beneficence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>To ‘do good’, health professionals should reflect on patient care decisions with this question in mind: ‘Will this treatment benefit the patient?’ This requires considering risks versus benefits and deciding whether the benefits outweigh the risks.</li>
<li>Often, families will not want a patient to know their diagnosis, or perhaps want the patient cared for in hospital rather than at home. The healthcare worker needs to be familiar with both the patient and their situation in order to make a good assessment and so guide the patient and family.</li>
<li>Knowing the patient’s wishes is essential in deciding what will benefit them.</li>
<li>The patient needs truthful information from the health professional in order to make his or her own decisions.</li>
<li>Although palliative care is concerned with the care of both the patient and the family, the healthcare worker must be careful not to subordinate the interest of the patients to the anxieties of relatives.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>It is often the case that family members want ‘everything’ done for their patient, not appreciating that, in fact, prolonging the patient’s life with artificial hydration or other measures only acts to prolong their suffering.</li>
<li>Families need to be informed about the consequences of different treatments and they also need to be encouraged to listen to what the patient wants.</li>
<li>With regard to children, parents are presumed to have the ability to make decisions regarding a child’s ‘best interests’.</li>
<li>In general, the best interests of a child are presumed to be life preserving, but in the face of irreversible illness this presumption requires careful exploration. ‘Best interests’ may require a plan that focuses on the child’s need for comfort and symptom relief to ease the process of dying in a way that promotes the safety, comfort and dignity of the child.</li>
<li>Palliative care is neither about shortening life nor prolonging the dying period.</li>
<li>It is of great importance that the health care professional establish, with the patient and the patient’s family, the clinical goals at which they are aiming.</li>
<li>One patient may desire only to die comfortably without pain, while another may want to live long enough to witness a particular event, a wedding, a birth etc. These different goals help the health worker to determine the best treatment decision to make in the case of each patient.</li>
<li>In order to ‘do good’, it is essential that the healthcare worker have the necessary degree of competence to address the various components of human suffering.</li>
<li>Health workers engaged in palliative care need to keep up-to-date with current knowledge in their field to be able to make judgements about the risks of interventions versus the benefits.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="non-maleficence">Non-maleficence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#non-maleficence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>To ‘do no harm’, a health worker needs to have adequate education and knowledge to ensure that the treatment they offer will not harm or endanger a patient.</li>
<li>In practice, many treatments carry some risk of harm and it is up to the health worker to weigh the risks versus the benefits.</li>
<li>The principle of non-maleficence is not absolute, and must be balanced against the principle of beneficence (doing good – see above).</li>
<li>In essence, the intended effect of treatment must be a good one. For instance: Artificial hydration at the end of life may appear to families to be good for a patient who is no longer taking oral fluids, whereas in fact it can cause great distress since the patient’s body can no longer cope with these fluids.</li>
<li>The principle of ‘double effect’ forbids the achievement of good ends by wrong means. It forbids doctors to relieve the distress of a dying patient by killing them, but it permits the use of drugs that relieve the distress of dying even when they may hasten death.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="justice">Justice<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#justice" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Justice is concerned with the correct use of resources and this is particularly pertinent when working in resource-poor settings such as in many areas of Africa.</li>
<li>It is the principle by which competing claims may be decided in fairness and is concerned with fair distribution of resources. Here are two examples:</li>
</ul>
<p>If a family has very limited resources, should the terminal patient be prescribed expensive treatment that will cause financial hardship for the rest of the family? In the days before anti-retrovirals became free or more affordable, individuals sometimes sold all the families property to buy drugs for a limited period of time. The family was eventually left impoverished.</p>
<p>&gt; Should patients with terminal cancer be started on ART? This is often seen with HIV-positive women being started on ART when they have advanced cancer of the cervix. In places where ARVs are scarce, this is a case where drugs would be better used for patients who have a longer prognosis.</p>
<p>This is also an example of futile treatment: ART is not going to have any curative effect on the cancer and in fact may prolong the woman’s suffering without just cause.</p>
<p>&gt; Prolonging the dying process may be justifiable if the patient and the family need that extra time to achieve important personal goals. However, the availability of medical technologies is not of itself an ethical command that these technologies be used.</p>
<p><strong>NB:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Ethics in palliative care is a matter of practical reasoning about individual patients, specific cases and unique situations.</li>
<li>What may be ethical in one case may be unethical in another.</li>
<li>A health professional at the bedside must understand the clinical situation in all its subtle medical and human complexity.</li>
<li>The fundamental moral attitudes orienting the contemporary practice of palliative care are an unconditional respect for the dignity of each person and the acceptance of finitude</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Role of various health-care providers</strong></p>
<p>Health-care providers involved in palliative care may include physicians, nurses, social workers, psychologists, spiritual counsellors, volunteers, pharmacists and traditional healers. Each can play a useful role as following:</p>
<p><strong>Physicians</strong></p>
<p>Physicians play a crucial role in interdisciplinary palliative care. They must be competent in general medicine, competent in control of pain and other symptoms, and must also be familiar with the principles of management of the patient’s disease. Physicians working in palliative care may be responsible for assessment, supervision and management of many of the difficult treatment dilemmas. They may also be</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>responsible for leading the interdisciplinary teams. They clearly play an important educational role as they discuss medical management decisions, and should regard research, and the critical application of research findings, as an integral part of their work.</p>
<p>In countries where there are as few as one physician to 50 000 people, it is unrealistic to have this scarce resource devoted totally to palliative care. By incorporating palliative care into the services provided by all doctors dealing with cancer patients (gynaecologists, surgeons, radiotherapists, paediatricians etc.), as well as including it in the undergraduate curriculum, wider, but less expert coverage can be obtained.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nurses">Nurses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nurses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The nurse is the team member who will typically have the greatest contact with the patient. This prolonged contact gives the nurse a unique opportunity to know the patient and the caregivers, to assess in depth what is happening and what is of importance to the patient, and to assist the patient to cope with the effects of advancing disease. The nurse's expertise in providing physical and emotional care to the patient, symptom management, patient and family education and in organizing the patient's environment to minimize loss of control, is critical to palliative care. Nurses are able to work closely with patients and families to make appropriate referrals to other disciplines and health-care services. In some settings, where the number of doctors is small, nurses may lead the multidisciplinary team and be responsible for the provision of all aspects of palliative care. In such settings, nurses need specialist training that is appropriate to the cultural and economic circumstances in which they work. Nurses in many settings play a role in providing public and volunteer education about palliative care.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="social-workers-and-psychologists">Social workers and psychologists<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#social-workers-and-psychologists" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The role of the social worker or psychologist is to help the family and patient deal with the personal and social problems of illness and disability, as well as to provide support during the progression of the disease and the bereavement process if the patient is at the end of life. The social worker's assessment helps define the patient's and family's needs from a psychosocial perspective, and helps anticipate problems within the family that may result from dysfunction and financial difficulties, particularly as the family begin planning for the future. Social work and psychology are both instrumental services. They may offer such interventions as referral to needed community services, emotional support (including individual counselling of patients and family members) and bereavement counselling.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="spiritual-counsellors">Spiritual counsellors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#spiritual-counsellors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The spiritual counsellor should be a skilled and non-judgemental listener, able to handle questions related to the meaning of life. Such questions invariably arise for patients and their families. The role of the spiritual counsellor is often one of listening, to facilitate recollection of the past and growing readiness for what lies ahead. The spiritual counsellor also often serves as a confidant and source of support for those with a</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>27</p>
<p>religious tradition, organizing religious rituals and sacraments that are meaningful to them. Spiritual counsellors need to be trained in end-of-life care.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="volunteers">Volunteers<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#volunteers" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The role of the volunteer within the palliative care team will vary according to the setting. In some low- or middle-resource settings, volunteers may provide most of the care for the patients. Volunteers are included in hospice and palliative care teams with the aim of assisting health-care professionals to provide the optimal quality of life for patients and families. Volunteers come from all sectors of the community, and often provide a link between health-care institutions and patients. Incorporating volunteers in a palliative care team brings in a dimension of community support and community expertise. With the appropriate training and support, volunteers can provide direct service to patients and families, help with administrative tasks, or even work as counsellors. They can also take on several other roles, such as raising awareness, providing health education, generating funds, helping with rehabilitation, or even delivering some types of medical care.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pharmacists">Pharmacists<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pharmacists" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Drug therapies are a major component of symptom management in palliative care, so the pharmacist plays an important role. The pharmacist ensures that patients and families have access to the essential drugs for palliative care. The pharmacist's expertise is also needed to support the health-care team by providing information on drug doses, drug interactions, appropriate formulations, routes of administration, and alternative approaches.</p>
<p>Morphine and other suitable medicines are necessary for palliative care. In many low- and middle-income countries, access to medicines is limited not only by the general lack of pharmacists to dispense medications, but also by the relatively high cost of medicines which makes them unaffordable for many cancer patients. For these reasons, pharmacists, even those with fairly basic skills and limited training are vital to palliative care service delivery. They can constitute analgesic medicines from the starting materials, like morphine powder, which is very cheap. Furthermore they can play a role in the management of an adequate distribution system for home care patients.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="traditional-healers">Traditional healers<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#traditional-healers" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The role of traditional medicine and traditional healers is well recognized. Worldwide, about two thirds of cancer patients resort to some form of complementary or alternative therapy (Ott, 2002). In most settings, traditional healers do not usually become members of the palliative care teams. Nevertheless, there should be scope for an open discourse between health-care providers and traditional healers with a view to coordinate their efforts to address the needs of patients and their families, in a sensitive and respectful way, taking into account the diverse cultures of communities and individuals.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="key-messages">Key messages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#key-messages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Palliative care is an urgent humanitarian need worldwide for people with cancer and other chronic fatal diseases. Palliative care is particularly needed in places where a high proportion of patients present in advanced stages and there is little chance of cure.</li>
<li>Ideally, palliative care services should be provided from the time of diagnosis of life-threatening illness, adapting to the increasing needs of cancer patients and their families as the disease progresses into the terminal phase. They should also provide support to families in their bereavement.</li>
<li>Effective palliative care services are integrated into the existing health system at all levels of care, especially community and home-based care. They involve the public and the private sector and are adapted to the specific cultural, social and economic setting.</li>
<li>In order to respond to the cancer priority needs in a community and make the best use of scarce resources, palliative care services should be strategically linked to cancer prevention, early detection and treatment services for both adults and children</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pain-and-pain-assessment">Pain and pain assessment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pain-and-pain-assessment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pain">Pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="definition">Definition:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>An unpleasant sensory and emotional experience associated with actual or potential tissue damage or described in terms of such damage</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="impact-of-pain-on-patient">Impact of pain on patient<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#impact-of-pain-on-patient" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Reduced quality of life</li>
<li>Induce depression</li>
<li>Exacerbated anxiety</li>
<li>Interfere with social performance and impair the quality of relationships</li>
<li>Impact negatively on physical capability</li>
<li>Prevent work and reduce income</li>
<li>Challenge existence belief</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="causes-of-pain">Causes of Pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#causes-of-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The illness itself, e.g. bony, visceral or soft-tissue involvement or nerve compression</li>
<li>Complications of the illness, e.g. pressure sores, constipation or post-herpetic neuralgia</li>
<li>Treatment of the illness, e.g. neuropathy caused by chemotherapy or ART</li>
<li>Co-morbidities, e.g. sickle cell disease.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="physiology-of-pain">Physiology of Pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#physiology-of-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Pain pathways involve the peripheral nervous system and central nervous system.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>The sensation of pain is made up of an initial fast, sharp pain and a later slow, dull, long-lasting pain and this is due to the difference in the speed of the nerve impulses in the different nerve-fibre types.</li>
<li>When cellular damage occurs, a number of chemical substances are produced or released which influence the degree of nerve activity and therefore the intensity of the pain sensation.</li>
<li>Pain from internal organs is perceived at a location that is not the source of the pain i.e. referred pain.</li>
<li>Chronic pain can result in an altered perception to pain, leading to increased sensitivity or abnormal sensations such as burning or numbness.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="definition-of-pain-terms">Definition of pain terms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definition-of-pain-terms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="acute-pain">Acute pain:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#acute-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Pain due to a definable acute injury or illness</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="allodynia">Allodynia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#allodynia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Pain caused by a stimulus which does not normally provoke pain</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="analgesia">Analgesia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#analgesia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Absence of pain in response to stimulation which would normally be painful</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="breakthrough-pain">Breakthrough pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#breakthrough-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>A transitory exacerbation of pain that occurs on a background of otherwise stable and controlled pain</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="causalgia">Causalgia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#causalgia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>A syndrome of sustained burning pain, allodynia and hyperpathia after a traumatic nerve lesion, often combined with vasomotor dysfunction and later trophic changes</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="central-pain">Central pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#central-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Pain associated with a lesion in the central nervous system (brain and spinal cord)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="chronic-pain">Chronic pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chronic-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Results from a chronic pathological process</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="dysaesthesia">Dysaesthesia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dysaesthesia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>An unpleasant abnormal sensation which can be either spontaneous or provoked</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="hyperaesthesia">Hyperaesthesia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hyperaesthesia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>An increased sensitivity to stimulation</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hyperalgesia">Hyperalgesia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hyperalgesia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>An increased response to a stimulus that is normally painful</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hyperpathia">Hyperpathia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hyperpathia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>A painful syndrome characterized by an increased reaction to a stimulus, especially a repetitive stimulus and an increased threshold</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="incident-pain">Incident pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#incident-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Occurs only in certain circumstances, such as after a particular movement or on standing: it should be regarded as chronic pain but, as it is intermittent, it is better managed with local measures where possible</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="neuralgia">Neuralgia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#neuralgia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Pain in the distribution of a nerve</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="neuropathy">Neuropathy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#neuropathy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>A disturbance of function or pathological change in a nerve</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="neuropathic-pain">Neuropathic pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#neuropathic-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Pain which is transmitted by a damaged nervous system, and which is usually only partially opioid-sensitive</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nociceptor">Nociceptor<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nociceptor" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>A receptor preferentially sensitive to a noxious stimulus or to a stimulus which would become noxious if prolonged</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nociceptive-pain">Nociceptive pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nociceptive-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Pain which is transmitted by an undamaged nervous system and is usually opioid-responsive</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pain-threshold">Pain threshold<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pain-threshold" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The least experience of pain which a subject can recognize</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pain-tolerance-level">Pain tolerance level<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pain-tolerance-level" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The greatest level of pain which a subject is prepared to tolerate</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="procedural-pain">Procedural pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedural-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Pain related to procedures / interventions: this is especially important in children with chronic illnesses. E.g HIV and malignancies and is an important cause of anxiety in children that can be prevented</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sympathetic-pain">Sympathetic pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sympathetic-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Pain caused by damage to sympathetic nerves</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-pain">Types of pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Pain can be classified according to:</p>
<ol>
<li>Underlying mechanism</li>
<li>Duration</li>
<li>Situation</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nb">NB:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nb" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Different types of pain respond differently to different types of analgesia, hence the importance for clinicians to determine the type of pain that a patient is experiencing in order to prescribe the most appropriate analgesia</li>
<li>Patients with life-threatening illnesses will often have both nociceptive and neuropathic pain and many will also have more than one cause of pain</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pain-due-to-underlying-mechanism">Pain due to underlying mechanism<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pain-due-to-underlying-mechanism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-nociceptive-pain">I) Nociceptive pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-nociceptive-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This type of pain is produced by stimulation of specific sensory receptors in the viscera and somatic structures (the nerves are intact). Its characteristics are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Somatic pain: superficial (cutaneous) in skin, subcutaneous tissue or mucous membranes: sharp and well localised pain, deep muscles, tendons, joints: more diffuse and dull</li>
<li>Visceral pain from organs: dull and poorly localised i.e the sensation of pain may be referred to a cutaneous site, · often associated with autonomic responses (e.g. sweating, nausea).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ii-neuropathic-pain">ii) Neuropathic pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-neuropathic-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This type of pain is produced by damage to the central or peripheral nervous system (the nerves are abnormal). Its characteristic are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Burning pain or shooting pain</li>
<li>Aching sensation relieved by pressure applied to the affected area</li>
<li>Increased sensitivity to a pain stimulus or to a stimulus that is not normally painful</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pain-described-in-terms-of-duration">Pain described in terms of duration<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pain-described-in-terms-of-duration" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>i) Acute</p>
<ul>
<li>This pain is associated with E.g tissue damage, inflammation, brief disease process, surgical procedure</li>
<li>The time is limited to a brief duration of hours, days, weeks or few months</li>
<li>Also serves as a warning of wrong happening.</li>
</ul>
<p>ii) Chronic</p>
<ul>
<li>Chronic pain can also be termed as persistent</li>
<li>Worsens and intensify with passage of time</li>
<li>Last for extended period of months, years or lifetime</li>
<li>Adversely affect patients functions or wellbeing</li>
<li>Classified as chronic malignant or chronic non malignant</li>
<li>May be related to E.g primary or metastatic process of a disease, treatment, side effects of drugs or infections</li>
<li>Common in HIV/AIDS, Arthritis or degenerative joint diseases, chronic obstructive pulmonary diseases, heart failures, neurological disorders(CVA, multiple sclerosis, fibromyalgia), sickle cell disease, cystic fibrosis, and diabetes</li>
<li>Associated with pain that fails to resolve within an expected time E.g. backache, trauma, spinal cord injury, reflex sympathetic dystrophy, or phantom pains</li>
</ul>
<p>Classification of chronic pain in pathophysiological terms</p>
<p>This is according to American Geriatric Association</p>
<ul>
<li>Norciceptive</li>
<li>Visceral or somatic resulting from stimulation of pain receptors</li>
<li>Neuropathic</li>
<li>Caused by peripheral or Central nerves system stimulation</li>
<li>Mixed (unspecified) pain</li>
<li>unknown pain mechanism</li>
</ul>
<p>Pain due to psychological disorders</p>
<p>Pain due to situation</p>
<ul>
<li>Breakthrough – a transitory exacerbation of pain that occurs on a background of otherwise controlled pain.</li>
<li>Incident pain – occurs only in certain circumstances (e.g. after a particular movement).</li>
<li>Procedural pain – related to procedures or intervention</li>
</ul>
<p>Other factors that contribute to pain perception</p>
<p>A) Psychological factors</p>
<ul>
<li>Pain is influenced by psychological factors as it affects the human consciousness</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Psychological factors are just as important in managing pain as the physical cause of the pain.</li>
<li>Psychological pain related to chronic pain often shows itself as depression or anxiety.</li>
<li>Distress associated with chronic pain may present as anger, frustration, hopelessness, denial, grief, sadness or withdrawal.</li>
<li>Avoiding activities and social contact affects the patient themselves and leads to less activity, more social isolation and a focus on the pain leading to:</li>
<li>a vicious circle of pain</li>
<li>lack of activity</li>
<li>fear</li>
<li>depression</li>
<li>more pain</li>
</ul>
<p>It has been shown that negative feelings, such as rejection or loss create neuronal stimulation patterns similar to those created by noxious stimuli.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="b-spiritual-factors">B) Spiritual factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-spiritual-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Spiritual and existential distress may manifest themselves in physical problems and are an important source of clinical suffering that can aggravate and even cause pain.</li>
<li>Spiritual pain may or may not have a religious component and often reflects the patient questioning of the meaning of life generally and that of his or her own life in particular.</li>
<li>Hopelessness and despair make pain difficult to bear and a sense of peace and strength from faith will help to make pain easier to live with.</li>
<li>Spiritual pain may prompt in the patient a re-evaluation of their life that lead to the recovery of values and beliefs. This maybe a transition point along their journey towards greater self-understanding.</li>
<li>Recognition and support for spiritual issues is an integral part of pain assessment and management.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="c-cultural-factors">C) Cultural factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#c-cultural-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Cultural factors play a major role in how we view health and illness and therefore pain.</li>
<li>A sensitive approach to culture, ethnicity and language will prevent the aggravation of pain and help reduce emotional distress.</li>
<li>Many cultures believe in ‘supernatural’ powers that can cause pain.</li>
<li>Different cultures respond differently to pain and it is important to recognise the different behaviours such as shouting and crying, or being stoical.</li>
<li>How we see the family and community respond to pain will affect how we as individuals respond to pain.</li>
<li>Language may also be a challenge, with the patient being unable to communicate effectively with the health professional and visa versa.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="d-social-factors">D) Social factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#d-social-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Unresolved social factors can aggravate pain and management of such issues can facilitate pain control</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Factors influencing pain may include the lack of aids for daily living, the lack of accessibility to community and local resources and services, along with financial and legal issues.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="principles-of-management-of-pain">Principles of management of pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#principles-of-management-of-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The management of pain is based on the type and cause of pain and needs to be holistic.</li>
<li>It is important to treat the underlying cause of the pain if it is treatable (e.g. an opportunistic infection).</li>
<li>The aims of pain management are:</li>
<li>Prompt relief of pain</li>
<li>Prevention of recurrence.</li>
<li>In the management of pain, the goals are for the patient to be pain free at night, then at rest during the day, and then pain free on movement.</li>
<li>It is important to discourage the acceptance of pain by health care workers as well as the patient and their family.</li>
<li>Both pharmacological and non-pharmacological methods should be used to manage pain.</li>
<li>Pain can be managed across a range of settings, including the home. It is only in severe cases where an individual may need to be hospitalized in order to get their pain under control.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pain-assessment">Pain Assessment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pain-assessment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>As pain is subjective, self-reporting is central to assessment. Comprehensive clinical assessment is vital for effective pain management.</li>
<li>A standard assessment guideline for pain is important to measure change over time.</li>
<li>Several pain assessment tools are available, for instance:</li>
<li>Body diagrams to document the site of pain and pain, rating scales to follow pain and the effect of treatment (which are useful in managing difficult pain).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="goals-of-pain-assessment">Goals of pain assessment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#goals-of-pain-assessment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>To understand the experience of the patient and the underlying holistic factors and pathophysiology contributing to the pain</li>
<li>To prevent the onset of detrimental effects (both psychological and physical) as a result of untreated pain.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="goals-of-measuring-pain">Goals of measuring pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#goals-of-measuring-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>To determine the presence, intensity and duration of pain</li>
<li>To determine the location of pain</li>
<li>To determine treatment efficacy.</li>
</ul>
<p>NB:</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Assessment should not be confused with measurement, where a score is indicative of pain intensity and treatment efficacy.</li>
<li>All findings or relevant information should be clearly documented.</li>
<li>Of particular interest would be information obtained during initial assessment, pain scores, intervention and intervention efficacy.</li>
<li>Clear and precise notes should be kept in a place easily accessible to other health professionals involved in caring for the patient.</li>
<li>Several pain assessment and measurement tools are available, e.g. body diagrams to document the site of pain; and pain rating scales to follow the patient's pain and the effect of treatment (useful in managing difficult pain).</li>
</ul>
<p>Use the PQRST tool for questions to assess pain: The PQRST of Pain is a good tool for organizing an assessment</p>
<ul>
<li>P- Precipitating and relieving factors: What makes your pain better/worse?</li>
<li>Q - Quality of pain: How would you describe your pain?</li>
<li>R - Radiation of pain: Is the pain in one place or does it move?</li>
<li>S - Site and severity of pain: Where is your pain? How bad is it?</li>
<li>T - Timing and previous treatment: How often do you get the pain? When? Are you on pain treatment? Does it help?</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="assessment-of-pain-requires-four-steps">Assessment of pain requires Four steps:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#assessment-of-pain-requires-four-steps" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="step-1-determine-the-nature-and-possible-causes-of-pain">Step 1: Determine the nature and possible causes of pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-1-determine-the-nature-and-possible-causes-of-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Identifying the aetiology of pain is essential to its management.</li>
<li>Prompt diagnosis and treatment of these syndromes can reduce morbidity associated with unrelieved pain. In the great majority of patients, the history, physical examination and, occasionally, an x-ray, are adequate to appropriately assess the pain.</li>
<li>In most cases, the pain is caused by direct tumour involvement.</li>
<li>Psychological, cultural, and chemical addiction factors can further influence a patient's pain experience.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="step-2-measuring-pain-intensity">Step 2: Measuring pain intensity<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-2-measuring-pain-intensity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>This assists in understanding the underlying pathology.</li>
<li>Certain types of pain such as neuropathic pain and incidental pain can be difficult to control and may require higher doses of opioids, trials of different opioids or the addition of appropriate adjuvant analgesics.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="step-3-perform-a-multidimensional-assessment">Step 3: Perform a multidimensional assessment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-3-perform-a-multidimensional-assessment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Terminally ill patients need to be assessed regularly since symptoms can change rapidly.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Keep in mind that there are three steps in the pain experience:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Production of pain: This occurs at the site of the cancer. This cannot be measured directly and can be (nociception) different from cancer to cancer, site to site, etc.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Perception: This occurs at the level of the central nervous system/brain. This component too, cannot be measured and is also subject to the influence of modulation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Expression: The expression of pain is the main target of all our assessments and treatment. Two patients with the same level of perception may express dramatically different pain intensity. Therefore, we should not equate the intensity of pain expression directly with nociception. Doing this would be a one-dimensional approach that ignores the complexity of the pain experience.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Appropriate pain assessment requires us to consider the multiple dimensions of certain patient’s expression of pain</p>
<p>Some of the components of a multi-dimensional pain assessment include:</p>
<p>a) Pain syndrome</p>
<p>Type of pain</p>
<ul>
<li>Location, radiation, intensity (use scale), triggers</li>
<li>Bone pain</li>
<li>Visceral pain</li>
<li>Neuropathic pain</li>
<li>Incidental</li>
<li>Are there other symptoms that need controlling?</li>
</ul>
<p>b) Drug</p>
<p>Dosage</p>
<ul>
<li>Are there indications of tolerance?</li>
<li>Are there signs of toxicity?</li>
<li>What has been the response to individual opioids?</li>
<li>What other treatments have been/are being used for pain relief?</li>
<li>Effectiveness?</li>
</ul>
<p>c) Patient</p>
<p>Underlying metabolic abnormalities (e.g. renal impairment, hypercalcemia, hepatic encephalopathy, etc)</p>
<ul>
<li>Is there significant psychological distress?</li>
<li>How has the patient coped previously with life stressors?</li>
<li>Is there a history of drug/alcohol addiction? Is the patient cognitively impaired / delirious? (Use screening tools such as Folstein MMSQ to assess cognition)</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Are there spiritual issues that need to be addressed (e.g. what is the meaning of pain to the patient?)
d) Social</li>
<li>Influence of pain on the patient’s daily living</li>
<li>What are the family and social support systems?</li>
<li>Is there severe family dysfunction?</li>
<li>Are there financial concerns?</li>
<li>Are there cultural issues influencing the illness experience?</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="step-4-identify-poor-prognostic-factors">Step 4: Identify poor prognostic factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-4-identify-poor-prognostic-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The following are ‘red flags’ to alert the possibility that difficulties may be encountered in trying to control pain;</p>
<ul>
<li>Neuropathic pain</li>
<li>Incidental pain (pain severely exacerbated by an incident such as movement, coughing, etc.).</li>
<li>Impaired cognitive functioning.</li>
<li>Major psychological distress.</li>
