<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="causes">Causes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#causes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Pressure on the upper airways by extrinsic compression caused by such things as enlarged lymph nodes or primary tumour in the lungs, head or neck</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>If impending obstruction is diagnosed, consider whether pre-emptive treatment with radiotherapy or tracheostomy is indicated.</li>
<li>Discuss the possible events with the patient and their family.</li>
<li>Offer sedation with morphine and benzodiazepines; 5–10mg morphine and 5–10mg diazepam given PO/SC/IV/PR depending on the patient’s condition.</li>
<li>In hospital, and if facilities are available and the condition of the patient allows, consider:</li>
<li>Bronchoscopy.</li>
<li>Chemotherapy</li>
</ul>
<p>g) Superior vena cava obstruction (SVCO)</p>
<ul>
<li>SVCO is the partial or complete obstruction of blood flow through the superior vena cava into the right atrium.</li>
<li>SVCO usually results in impairment of the venous return.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="causes">Causes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#causes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>External compression by tumour or lymph nodes, or thrombosis as a result of compression</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Dyspnoea</li>
<li>Facial/upper body including arms swell</li>
<li>Headaches</li>
<li>Visual changes</li>
<li>Unclear mind (muzziness)</li>
<li>Cough., Dysphagia.</li>
<li>Some patients may complain of a sensation of drowning.</li>
</ul>
<p>This condition is common in patients with tumours within the mediastinum, i.e. bronchial carcinoma, cancer of the breast and lymphoma.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="assessment">Assessment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#assessment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Examination may reveal engorged conjunctivae, periorbital oedema, dilated neck veins and the collateral veins on the arms and chest wall. Late signs include; pleural effusions, pericardial effusion and stridor.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>In advanced disease the patient needs relief of their acute symptoms.,</li>
<li>Give high-dose corticosteroids (e.g. dexamethasone 16mg PO/IV) and, if available, urgent radiotherapy</li>
<li>At the same time, treat dyspnoea symptomatically with morphine (5mgs 4hrly) and/or a benzodiazepine. Practical management of dyspnoea is also important – e.g., teach the patient how to breathe slowly, and, encourage a calm environment.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Without treatment, SVCO carries a very poor prognosis.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="h-choking">h) Choking<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#h-choking" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Choking is the inability to breathe as a result of acute obstruction of the pharynx, larynx or trachea.</p>
<p>This can be due to local tumour or neurological swallowing difficulties, as well as a more general obstruction.</p>
<p><strong>Assessment and management of choking from local tumours</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Acknowledge the patient’s and family’s fears</li>
<li>Discuss interventions truthfully with the patient and family.</li>
<li>High-dose steroids may be useful to reduce the swelling around the obstructing tumour</li>
<li>Palliative radiation, if available, may also help</li>
<li>Midazolam 5mg sc can help to sedate the patient and reduce anxiety</li>
<li>Rectal diazepam can be used, especially in the community.</li>
</ul>
<p>In children, especially with a sudden onset of choking, think about foreign bodies!</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="i-haemorrhage">i) Haemorrhage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-haemorrhage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Haemorrhage is profuse bleeding from one of the major blood vessels, e.g. the carotid artery.</p>
<ul>
<li>While uncommon, haemorrhage can be a frightening event for patient and carers.</li>
<li>Haemorrhage is, however, often predictable and needs to be proactively managed, e.g., make medicines available in the home care setting in case the possible emergency occurs.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Causes</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Catastrophic bleeding from a large blood vessel due to tumour erosion from areas such as the head and neck, stomach, pelvis, bladder or lungs.</li>
<li>Patients with cirrhosis may also have torrential bleeding from oesophageal varices.</li>
<li>Many patients also have disorders of their blood-clotting systems.</li>
<li>Low platelets associated with malignancies (bone marrow infiltration) and HIV.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Assessment and management</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Communicate gently and truthfully and stay with the patient.</li>
<li>Use dark cloths to soak up the blood.</li>
<li>Pack a bleeding nose with gauze and BIPP or use nasal tampons.</li>
<li>Adrenalin-soaked ribbon gauze may also help as it causes local vasoconstriction.</li>
<li>For small oral bleeds, crushed cyclocapron may help.</li>
<li>Sedation (such as diazepam 10mg PO/PR) is indicated if the patient is distressed.</li>
<li>Remember that if the bleeding is torrential, the blood pressure will drop quickly and so medications will not be effective.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>The family or staff of a patient having the likelihood of a haemorrhage should be counselled about that „ possibility and advised what they should do in such a situation as well as being offered support after the event if it occurs.</li>
</ul>
<p>j) Epistaxis (severe nose bleed)</p>
<ul>
<li>This is particularly prevalent in children with haematological malignancies.</li>
<li>In children, aim for rapid and complete sedation with benzodiazepines and/or opioids if available; use parenteral routes.</li>
<li>If able to swallow, give children double the usual dose of morphine with or without diazepam.</li>
<li>If unable to swallow, give large doses of morphine and diazepam rectally.</li>
<li>Rectal valium dose: If weight is not known: &lt;3years 5mg; &gt;3years up to 10mg., If weight known: 0.5–1mg/kg up to a maximum of 10kg</li>
</ul>
<p>THE DYING PROCESS</p>
<ul>
<li>This is a period of time when the body begins to shut down and prepare for death. It is an important moment of time for the dying person and their loved ones during which they can express their feelings and show their love</li>
</ul>
<p>Changes that maybe noticed during the dying process and what to do to help</p>
<p>&gt; Appetite and thirst</p>
<ul>
<li>The person’s appetite and thirst may decrease, and they may have little desire to eat or drink. This concerns many carers, but it is a natural process and it is not painful for the person.</li>
</ul>
<p>Remedy: Sips of water or a moist mouth swab will help provide comfort.</p>
<p>NB: Attempting to feed someone who is unable to swallow may make them distressed and cause harm.</p>
<p>&gt; Sleep and alertness</p>
<ul>
<li>Changes in the person’s body mean that they may spend a lot of time asleep, may be drowsy or difficult to wake up.</li>
</ul>
<p>Remedy: It is best to talk to the person when they seem most alert, and allow them to sleep when they want to. There is no need to shake the person or to speak loudly. It is best to speak softly and naturally.</p>
<p>&gt; Temperature</p>
<ul>
<li>The body’s temperature may change. Sometimes the person’s hands, feet and legs may be increasingly cool to the touch and at other times they may feel hot and clammy. Sometimes, parts of the person’s body may become blotchy and darker in colour. This is due to the circulation of the blood slowing down and is a normal part of the dying process.</li>
</ul>
<p>Remedy</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>If the person indicates that they feel cold, use light bedding to keep them warm.</li>
<li>Too many bed clothes or an electric blanket may make them hot and restless.</li>
<li>Provide good ventilation; a fan to circulate the air and cool damp towels can help if the person seems hot.</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; <strong>Breathing</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Regular breathing patterns may change. Sometimes the breathing may be fast and at other times there may be long gaps between breaths.</li>
<li>Breathing may be shallow or noisy. This is a normal part of the dying process and is not painful or distressing for the person.</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; <strong>Secretions</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>As coughing and swallowing reflexes slow down, saliva and mucus may collect in the back of the person’s throat, causing a gurgling, bubbling or other noises. These noises can be a concern to carers, but do not usually cause distress to the person.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Remedy</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>To help improve the situation, one can turn the person on their side or lift their head supported with pillows, so that their head is turned to one side in a comfortable position.</li>
<li>Medications can also be given to slow down the production of saliva and mucus and thus improve comfort.</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; <strong>Restlessness</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Due to the decrease in circulation of blood to the brain and to other changes happening in the body, the person may become restless or agitated.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Remedy</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>To have a calming effect, speak in a quiet natural way, lightly massage the person’s hand or forehead, or softly play familiar music.</li>
<li>Let a palliative care team member know if the person becomes restless or agitated.</li>
<li>Medications can be given to reduce the problem.</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; <strong>Incontinence</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>The amount of urine that the body is producing decreases due to the reduced amount of fluid the person is drinking. The urine may become stronger smelling and darker in colour.</li>
<li>Many carers are concerned that the person will lose control of their bladder and bowels.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Remedy</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>This does not happen to all people, but if it does, there are pads, easy to use equipment, and special absorbent sheets, to enhance comfort and hygiene.</li>
<li>A member of the palliative care team can advise of about what is needed.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>How to know that death has occurred</strong></p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Breathing stops</li>
<li>No heartbeat or pulse can be felt</li>
<li>The person cannot be woken up</li>
<li>Eyelids may be half open</li>
<li>Their mouth may be open</li>
<li>Pupils are fixed</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-process-of-grief">THE PROCESS OF GRIEF<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-process-of-grief" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Definition:</strong> Grief is a type of stress reaction, a highly personal and subjective response that an individual makes to a real perceived or anticipated loss</p>
<ul>
<li>Grief reaction may occur in any loss situation be it, physical or tangible loss, e.g. death, significant injury, property loss or symbolic and intangible loss e.g. a dream loss</li>
<li>The intensity of grief varies depending on many variables e.g. meaning of loss to the individual experiencing it</li>
<li>Grief is a process not a state</li>
<li>It takes time to work through</li>
<li>Grief is a natural response to a significant loss.</li>
<li>The most extreme of such losses is experienced through death of someone close with whom we had a meaningful relationship.</li>
<li>We can also experience significant grief through other losses, through divorce, separation, abortion and even our lifestyle or a job.</li>
</ul>
<p>However, the response to these losses is similar.</p>
<p><strong>Why death maybe most significant, this might be due to:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>The finality</li>
<li>Death confronts one’s own mortality or finiteness.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Grief will be dependent on a number of factors including:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Degree of attachment to the person lost.</li>
<li>Degree to which person enriched your life.</li>
<li>Quality and length of relationship.</li>
<li>Nature of death.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Other factors that may influence grief</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Understanding the Process of Grief</li>
<li>Common Individual Responses to Grief</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Normal reactions to grief:</strong></p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>i) Fear:</p>
<ul>
<li>Of one’s own mortality</li>
<li>For the safety of oneself or one’s family</li>
<li>Apparently unrelated fears Anger:</li>
<li>At the injustice and senselessness of it all</li>
<li>Generalised anger and irritability</li>
<li>How this could happen to such a good person</li>
</ul>
<p>ii) Sadness:</p>
<ul>
<li>About the loss both personally and professionally</li>
<li>Flash backs or triggers to previous grief and loss</li>
<li>About the loss of feeling safe and secure</li>
<li>Feeling depressed or “flat” for no reason Guilt:</li>
<li>For having appeared helpless or emotional</li>
<li>For not behaving as you would have liked</li>
<li>For unresolved issues or conflict with the person</li>
<li>For not doing upon reflection what you feel might have the process.</li>
</ul>
<p>Physical Sleep:</p>
<ul>
<li>Difficulty getting off to sleep because of intrusive thoughts</li>
<li>Restless and disturbed sleep</li>
<li>Feeling tired and fatigued Physical problems:</li>
<li>General agitation and muscle tension</li>
<li>Headaches or general aches and pains</li>
<li>Feeling generally unwell</li>
<li>Other physical signs and symptoms</li>
</ul>
<p>Thinking memories:</p>
<ul>
<li>Frequent thoughts or images of the person</li>
<li>Flashbacks or feelings of “reliving” past grief and loss experiences</li>
<li>Attempts to shut-out the painful memories</li>
<li>Real or imagined pictures of what happened “jumping” into your head E.g</li>
</ul>
<p>i) Dreams</p>
<ul>
<li>Dreams and nightmares about what happened</li>
<li>Unpleasant dreams of other frightening thoughts</li>
</ul>
<p>ii) Confusion</p>
<ul>
<li>Difficulty making simple decisions</li>
<li>Inability to concentrate and memory problems</li>
<li>Not being able to express yourself fluently verbally</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Feeling confused generally</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="behaviour-social">Behaviour social:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#behaviour-social" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Withdrawal from others and a need to be alone</li>
<li>Easily irritated by other people</li>
<li>Feelings of detachment from others</li>
<li>Loss of interest in normal activities and hobbies such as:</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-work">i) Work<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-work" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Not wanting to go to work, poor motivation</li>
<li>Poor concentration and attention</li>
<li>Sense of lost purpose in your work</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ii-habits">ii) Habits<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-habits" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Increased use of alcohol, cigarettes or other drugs</li>
<li>Loss of appetite or increased eating</li>
<li>Loss of interest in enjoyable activities</li>
</ul>
<p>Some most important needs of the person experiencing grief can be summarised as follows:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="i-to-feel-support">i) To feel support<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-to-feel-support" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>We need to recognise that bereavement raises all sorts of feelings, and that to cope people need the care, love, support and understanding of others.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="ii-to-face-reality">ii) To face reality<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-to-face-reality" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>The process of recovery is linked directly to acceptance of the loss.</li>
<li>Acceptance will not come until reality is faced.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="iii-to-express-feelings-emotional-release">iii) To express feelings (emotional release).<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-to-express-feelings-emotional-release" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>It seems important to many grieving people that emotional release be encouraged.</li>
<li>Grieving people should be helped, encouraged and allowed to express their feelings. This may mean tears, talking and other acting out.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nb">NB:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nb" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Too often “would be helpers” suppress rather than encourage and facilitate emotional release.</li>
<li>In some communities, people often get uncomfortable if you demonstrate your reaction to loss and pain openly and they may have some comments such as:</li>
<li>“Buck-up”</li>
<li>“It’s time to get over it …”</li>
<li>“It’s God’s will.”</li>
<li>“Brighten-up, you’re upsetting the team.”</li>
</ul>
<p>Although these comments are well-intentioned, they may prevent one from expressing his/her feelings. We need to recognise that “being upset” is healthy and helpful rather than harmful.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>iv) To move towards the re-establishment of self (on going living).</p>
<ul>
<li>For many this is difficult and for a period of time, often many months, painful and at times unthinkable.</li>
<li>Grieving people need all the care, encouragement and support they can get to enable on-going living to be increasingly possible.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="seeking-extra-support-and-assistance">Seeking extra Support and Assistance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#seeking-extra-support-and-assistance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Some people benefit from extra help in overcoming the effects of grief and loss.</li>
<li>One should not be reticent to get help if one thinks he/she needs it. This is not a sign of weakness or an indication that one is not in control.</li>
<li>Often, the help one receives will be short and simple and will prevent you from having longer term problems.</li>
</ul>
<p>One may need further assistance if:</p>
<ul>
<li>The problems are particularly severe, or if they continue for more than five or six weeks.</li>
<li>One feels numb or empty and do not have appropriate feelings, one may find her/himself keeping busy all the time in order to avoid the unpleasant thoughts and feelings.</li>
<li>One has no friends / family to whom one can talk about the experience and how one feels</li>
<li>One is using alcohol or drugs to help he/she cope.</li>
<li>If one has any other concerns about the way he/she or colleagues are coping and would like to discuss the matter.</li>
<li>If one feels he/she would benefit from further support consider accessing the People Assist (EAP) Program or speaking to a doctor/ nurse/health care provider</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="summary">Summary<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#summary" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Some DO’s and DON’TS for assisting others through the Grief Process</p>
<p>a. DO listen and hear what is being said.
b. DO acknowledge that each person’s experience of grief and loss will be unique.
c. DO remember that nobody has to justify their feelings to you.
d. DO realise the person has suffered a loss, even if you don’t perceive it as such.
e. DO allow the person time to grieve.
f. DO encourage those who have a common grief to support one another if they can.
g. DO encourage the griever to express emotion and to work through the grief.
h. DO remember that many who give support immediately after a death may be delayed in working through their own grief.
i. DO recognise the intensity of grief that will be experienced at certain significant times, eg, anniversaries, Christmas functions.
j. DON’T personalise knock-backs to your efforts to assist.
k. DON’T prevent the person from expressing their guilt or anger if they need to.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol>
<li>DON'T stifle the person’s desire to talk about the deceased
m. DON'T give trite answers to the ‘Why?’ question.</li>
</ol>
<p>In conclusion as far as grief is concerned:</p>
<p>Be Aware: Remember that working through grief is a normal and necessary process.</p>
<p>Be There: Learn to be with the person, not to solve the problem.</p>
<p>Be Sensitive: Allow the distress and do not try to take it away.</p>
<p>Be Human: Allow expression of feelings (guilt, anger, sorrow, depression) without judgement.</p>
<p>Be Ready: To listen when the story is told over and over again.</p>
<p>Be Patient: Remember that the process of mourning takes time.</p>
<p>BEREAVEMENT AND STAGES / PHASES OF GRIEF</p>
<p>Bereavement</p>
<ul>
<li>This is the period of mourning or state of intense grief especially following the death of a loved one or having lost something very dear</li>
</ul>
<p>Purpose</p>
<ul>
<li>Is not to let go of the deceased and move on, but to find ways to remember the dead person that are safe, healthy and less painful as time goes on (Thomas and Jay, 1997)</li>
</ul>
<p>Principles</p>
<ul>
<li>Helping the family, including children, to cope with grief and loss forms an integral part of palliative care.</li>
<li>A comprehensive assessment undertaken at initial contact with the family will provide information on family deaths, other losses, quality of relationships, coping mechanisms, depression, and any alcohol or substance abuse.</li>
</ul>
<p>Bereavement in general</p>
<ul>
<li>Bereavement is the normal process through which people adapt to a life without a loved one. However, complicated grief can lead to serious illness and requires professional intervention.</li>
<li>Bereavement is a complex mix of individual feelings (grief) and a process that we show to others in ways that are strongly influenced by environment, culture and society (mourning).</li>
<li>Feelings of grief during bereavement can be painful physically, socially, spiritually and emotionally.</li>
<li>Risk factors for complicated grief include multiple losses and dysfunctional relationships.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>There are three main areas relating to bereavement needs in palliative care:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Living with a life-threatening illness means facing loss of life and loss of a future.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Patients may have lost children, spouses and other relatives in the course of their lives from many causes. Emotional, social and spiritual care during the illness will explore bereavement for patient and family members so as to provide peace and resolution during the dying process.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Emotional, spiritual and practical support is provided to all family members who require help after the „death of the patient for as long as is necessary.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Periods of bereavement vary according to:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The manner of death (long illness, sudden death or traumatic death such as car accident, murder, medical mistake)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The age of the person who dies (a child’s death often feels out of place; an older person has often had „longer relationships)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The age of the bereaved (child development affects reaction; life stage is relevant)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Gender (women are often allowed more emotional expression than men)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Previous experiences of loss and their impact</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Support systems,</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Personal coping styles</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Family and cultural rules</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>In sub-Saharan Africa, the high incidence of cancer- and AIDS-related deaths means that many families experience multiple losses and hardships, such as unemployment, poverty and malnutrition, during their bereavement</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Adults and children react differently in bereavement because of different levels of understanding of death and the ability to express things differently at various stages of life. By the time late childhood is reached, most people understand that death is:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Permanent and irreversible (the body cannot be revived)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Unavoidable (death is inevitable and cannot be escaped)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Universal (death happens to everyone)</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="common-reactions-in-bereavement">Common reactions in bereavement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#common-reactions-in-bereavement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>There are physical, emotional, social and spiritual reactions to loss.</li>
<li>These are experienced in a complex mix and change over time.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="physical-reactions">Physical reactions:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#physical-reactions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Aches and pains</li>
<li>Nausea and/or vomiting</li>
<li>Headaches</li>
<li>Confusion</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>weakness and numbness</li>
<li>Change in sexual needs (loss/increase of libido)</li>
<li>Vulnerability to infections, cold, illness (low immunity)</li>
<li>Changes in eating and sleeping patterns</li>
<li>Shortness of breath</li>
<li>Dry mouth, Sweating</li>
<li>Frequent urination</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="emotional-reactions">Emotional reactions:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#emotional-reactions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Disbelief</li>
<li>Numbness</li>
<li>Sadness</li>
<li>Crying, even sobbing</li>
<li>Unexpected thoughts and feelings, often painful</li>
<li>Guilt</li>
<li>Panic and fear</li>
<li>Appearing distracted</li>
<li>Feelings of helplessness</li>
<li>Anger (at self and others)</li>
<li>Blame</li>
<li>Regret</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="social-reactions">Social reactions:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#social-reactions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Needing to say goodbye (stay with the body, view the body)</li>
<li>Interaction with people at public gathering, funeral</li>
<li>Selecting and undertaking rituals</li>
<li>Self-absorption and anti-social behaviour</li>
<li>Needing to talk of the deceased</li>
<li>A sense of isolation from the world ('in a bubble')</li>
<li>Attempting to carry on as usual (social face)</li>
<li>Needing to be alone or need to be with others.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="spiritual-reactions">Spiritual reactions:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#spiritual-reactions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Questioning why this has happened</li>
<li>Challenging the belief system (strengthening, decrease or change in beliefs)</li>
<li>Bargaining with a higher power</li>
<li>Talking to the deceased</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Dreams that may have significance about the deceased</li>
<li>Review of the meaning of life</li>
<li>Consideration of ending own life</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="bereavement-and-children">Bereavement and children<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bereavement-and-children" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Children’s reactions will depend on age, personal development and environment.</li>
<li>An understanding of the meaning of death changes as a child gets older:</li>
<li>Children 0–2 years of age (infants and toddlers)</li>
<li>Miss the physical contact, security and comfort if a main caregiver dies.</li>
<li>Show upset by changes in sleeping or eating patterns, crying or irritability, and becoming withdrawn.</li>
<li>When toddlers, may be angry and have tantrums or go back to baby behaviour.</li>
<li>Children 3–6 years of age</li>
<li>Are unable to understand that death is for ever and may ask when the dead person is coming back.</li>
<li>Confuse fact and fantasy and may think the death was caused by magic.</li>
<li>Grieve in bits and pieces, at times appearing to have forgotten about the death and able to play, happily, then becoming upset again.</li>
<li>Children 6–9 years of age:</li>
<li>Are able to understand that death is for ever (permanent) and it happens to everyone (universal) but still imagine that death is avoidable.</li>
<li>Are interested in practical matters, for example what happens to the dead person’s body.</li>
<li>May feel responsibility for causing the death because of behaviour or thoughts.</li>
<li>Children 9–12 years of age:</li>
<li>Have approximately the same understanding of death as adults: death will happen to everyone sooner or later (universal); no-one escapes it, including them (unavoidable); and death is for ever (permanent).</li>
<li>Understand that death can be sudden and can happen at any time (not just for the elderly and sick); may fear their own death.</li>
<li>Begin to think about the meaning of life and what happens after death.</li>
<li>Adolescents</li>
<li>Have as much understanding of death as adults.</li>
<li>May take risks to ‘test’ life</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-bereavement">Management of bereavement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-bereavement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Ideally, bereavement planning starts before the death of the patient.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Make contact as soon as possible to acknowledge the death and to offer condolences according to personal and cultural norms and customs.</li>
<li>Some help with documentation such as a will or death certificate may be needed.</li>
<li>Give information about the illness and the process of death if there is confusion about what happened.</li>
<li>Use simple explanations and avoid medical jargon.</li>
<li>Be there to listen, even when there is repetition the latter is necessary for bereaved people to accept the reality of the death.</li>
<li>Inform all family members about the bereavement process and what to expect physically, emotionally, socially and spiritually in this.</li>
<li>Do not discourage the bereaved from expressing sadness, depression or anger by trying to comfort.</li>
<li>These and other extreme emotions are all normal, although they can be distressing to witness. Allow periods of silence.</li>
<li>Encourage talk of the deceased as much as is needed.</li>
<li>Ask about the deceased and use their name to make it personal.</li>
<li>Share memories by asking questions such as: ‘Where did you meet?’, ‘What kind of child was she?’, ‘Can you tell me about him?’</li>
<li>Explore the spiritual needs of each family member and facilitate discussion, networking with a traditional healer or religious leader as required.</li>
<li>Do not assume faith will have remained the same as before the death.</li>
<li>Some people express the pain of grief in tears, which can be very helpful.</li>
<li>Others feel unable to cry easily. E.g. men are often discouraged from crying and it is important to help them find alternative forms of emotional release.</li>
<li>Physical activity is especially helpful, and may include walking, playing sports, hitting a punch bag, or gardening.</li>
<li>Encourage regular physical activity for all bereaved persons as this helps the mind and body to process emotional pain.</li>
<li>Suggest the family use journals and art, or create a memory book to share and express memories.</li>
<li>Do not offer platitudes. These include ‘It was for the best,’ or ‘He is in a better place now.’</li>
<li>Also, do not say, ‘I know exactly what you are going through,’ because you do not know that this is true and it may certainly not be how the bereaved person is feeling.</li>
<li>Bereavement can take a very long time, much longer than people imagine.</li>
<li>Let bereaved persons know to expect that they may perhaps still be struggling some months or years later. Prepare families for times that may be especially hard, such as birthdays, death anniversaries, family celebrations and school/university events.</li>
</ul>
<p>NB:</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Adults need to provide children with explanations, reassurance and the freedom to express emotions about · death and loss.</li>
<li>Encourage family members to include children in rituals, depending on cultural expectations.</li>
<li>Guide adults away from using euphemisms such as ‘she has gone to sleep’ or ‘he has gone away’, because · children will understand these literally.</li>
<li>Encourage family members to use drawing and play with children to assist them in expressing confusion and · pain at the loss.</li>
<li>Help adolescents to talk when it is convenient for them.</li>
<li>Often, a school friend or another adult will be chosen as a confidant.</li>
<li>Orphans may need extra bereavement care because of their living conditions, as well as the emotional burden · they bear without the support of close family elders.</li>
<li>Establish and strengthen child-to-child activities, such as support or discussion groups, a ‘buddy’ or friend · programme, sports and play activities, music, dancing and drama.</li>
<li>Child committees can plan these activities either with or without the help of adults.</li>
<li>Use traditional approaches to providing emotional support to children, E.g storytelling by elders and · mentoring.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="assessment-of-difficulties-in-bereavement">Assessment of difficulties in bereavement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#assessment-of-difficulties-in-bereavement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Occasionally, people have complications that prevent a healthy resolution of the bereavement process and that · require referral to a specialist.</li>
<li>A few indicators include:</li>
<li>After a couple of years the bereaved cannot speak of the deceased without experiencing intense and fresh grief each time.</li>
<li>Material possessions belonging to the deceased remain totally untouched as a shrine.</li>
<li>A history of depression which may make bereavement difficult to resolve.</li>
<li>Suicidal thoughts continue after a couple of years.,</li>
<li>Phobias about illness or death intrude upon the daily life of the bereaved.</li>
<li>A comprehensive psychosocial assessment and genogram of the family will highlight possible areas of concern.</li>
<li>Complicated bereavements should be referred, with the consent of the bereaved, to a health practitioner and, · where possible, someone trained in bereavement counselling.</li>
</ul>
<p>Risk factors for complicated grief include:</p>
<ul>
<li>multiple losses</li>
<li>dysfunctional relationships</li>
</ul>
<p>When a person dies, we say that their family and friends are ‘bereaved’. This means they have lost something precious to them and are grieving. Grieving may take place before death for instance -:</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>When someone is told they have an incurable illness</li>
<li>When told that their e.g. cancer treatment has not worked</li>
<li>When they become unable to care for themselves</li>
<li>When they and their family may grieve the loss of their independence, their health or their future.</li>
</ul>
<p>NB: “Suffering is not a problem that demands a solution, it is not a question that demands an answer; it is a mystery that demands a presence.”( John Wyatt, 1998)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mourning">Mourning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mourning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Mourning is often used interchangeably with grief, but mourning more specifically refers to the public expression of grief.</li>
<li>This public expression, perhaps crying or wailing does not necessarily relate to the significance of the loss, it is usually related to cultural and religious values and encourages social support for the mourner.</li>
<li>While we grieve internally we mourn externally</li>
<li>Complicated mourning arises from an interrupted or obstructed grief process and can result in potentially harmful outcomes, from somatic discomfort to chronic emotional distress and even the possibility of death</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-4-tasks-of-mourning">The 4 tasks of mourning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-4-tasks-of-mourning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>William Worden created a theory called “Tasks of Grief” which are the experiences that we go through after the death of someone we care about.</p>
<ol>
<li>To accept the reality of the Loss</li>
<li>To experience the pain of Grief</li>
<li>To adjust to the new environment where the deceased person is missing</li>
<li>To reinvest energy in life, loosen ties to the deceased and forge a new type of relationship with them based on memory, spirit and love</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="task-1-accept-the-reality-of-the-loss">Task 1: Accept the Reality of the Loss<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#task-1-accept-the-reality-of-the-loss" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The first task of mourning is to acknowledge and accept the reality that the person is dead and will not return.</p>
<ul>
<li>This can take time, as the bereaved often experience a period of numbness, shock, and disbelief even if the death was expected.</li>
<li>The bereaved may experience a period of searching or yearning for their loved one.</li>
<li>Some people report that they expect to see their loved one when they arrive at home, calling out before remembering that the person is gone.</li>
<li>Others find themselves continuing their old routine, such as setting the table for two.</li>
<li>The bereaved may even refer to the deceased loved one in the present tense or use the present and past tense together in the same conversation.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Traditional rituals such as funerals or memorial services can help people accomplish this task.</li>
<li>This public good-bye helps people confront the finality of the death.</li>
<li>Often the first visit to the cemetery reinforces the finality of the loss as well.</li>
<li>The primary aim of grief support at this task is to help people accept the reality of the loss in their own time and at their own pace. It may take days or weeks for the reality to be accepted, even longer for it to be fully absorbed.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="task-2-experience-the-pain-of-grief">Task 2: Experience the Pain of Grief<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#task-2-experience-the-pain-of-grief" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The second task of mourning is to express the pain of grief in a healthy way.</li>
<li>The pain of grief includes physical, emotional, behavioural, cognitive, spiritual, and social responses to the loss. This task is crucial because if the bereaved cannot, or does not, acknowledge the pain of grief in some way, these responses could manifest themselves in unhealthy ways.</li>
<li>Coping with the pain of grief is unique for each individual.</li>
<li>Sleep disturbances and appetite changes are the most common behavioural responses.</li>
<li>The cognitive responses to loss may be the primary way some people experience grief.</li>
<li>Initially many bereaved report a fear of ‘going crazy’, education and normalization of the grief process may help assuage this fear.</li>
<li>Spiritual responses to loss are also highly individual.</li>
<li>Even those who have a strong spiritual or religious belief system may not be comforted by their beliefs.</li>
<li>One of the crucial aspects of the spiritual response to loss is the ability to make meaning from the experience.</li>
<li>The social response to loss is often overlooked. But in AIDS-related deaths, people may be coping with social isolation due to stigmatization.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="task-3-adjust-to-the-loss">Task 3: Adjust to the Loss<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#task-3-adjust-to-the-loss" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The third task of mourning is to develop the skills and fill the roles needed to move forward without the physical presence of the deceased.</li>
<li>This can usually begin only after several months of dealing with the loss.</li>
<li>It may include adjusting to living alone, being a single parent, getting a job, learning to manage finances, or taking on household tasks.</li>
<li>For those dealing with HIV themselves, it can also mean needing to find other caregivers.</li>
<li>Part of adjusting to the loss is facing all the significant ‘firsts’ that occur in the first year of bereavement.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="task-4-reinvesting-energy">Task 4: Reinvesting Energy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#task-4-reinvesting-energy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>From the Deceased into New Life</li>
<li>The fourth and final task of mourning entails transferring the emotional energy invested in the relationship with the deceased into new, healthy approaches to life.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Reinvesting the emotional energy into new life may include gestures such as considering the deceased’s perspective in a difficult situation or wearing a locket with a picture of the deceased.</li>
<li>Others may reinvest this energy outward in socio-political activity such as becoming active in an AIDS organisation or creating a memorial for the deceased.</li>
<li>In general the signs that bereaved people are learning to accommodate their grief include:</li>
<li>A return to good health (or their health status before the death of their loved one)</li>
<li>Acknowledgment of the reality of the loss</li>
<li>Redefined identity</li>
<li>Emergence of new skills or roles</li>
<li>Establishment of or reconnection with a social support system</li>
<li>Ability to cope effectively with temporary upsurges of grief</li>
<li>Comfort with the quest to find meaning</li>
<li>Personal growth/transformation</li>
</ul>
<p>It is important to understand that those who grieve the death of a loved one never truly ‘get over’ the loss. Even after people accomplish the tasks of mourning, the pain of the loss is still present though hopefully less intense and more manageable (Worden, 1991)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="phases-of-grief">Phases of grief<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#phases-of-grief" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-denial">a) Denial<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-denial" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This first stage of grieving helps us to survive the loss. In this stage, the world becomes meaningless and overwhelming. Life makes no sense. We are in a state of shock and denial. We go numb. We wonder how we can go on, if we can go on, why we should go on. We try to find a way to simply get through each day.</p>
<p>Denial and shock help us to cope and make survival possible. Denial helps us to pace our feelings of grief. There is a grace in denial. It is nature’s way of letting in only as much as we can handle. As you accept the reality of the loss and start to ask yourself questions, you are unknowingly beginning the healing process. You are becoming stronger, and the denial is beginning to fade. But as you proceed, all the feelings you were denying begin to surface.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="b-anger">b) Anger<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-anger" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Anger is a necessary stage of the healing process. Be willing to feel your anger, even though it may seem endless. The more you truly feel it, the more it will begin to dissipate and the more you will heal. There are many other emotions under the anger and you will get to them in time, but anger is the emotion we are most used to managing. The truth is that anger has no limits. It can extend not only to your friends, the doctors, your family, yourself and your loved one who died, but also to God. You may ask, "Where is God in this?</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Underneath anger is pain, your pain. It is natural to feel deserted and abandoned, but we live in a society that fears anger. Anger is strength and it can be an anchor, giving temporary structure to the nothingness of loss. At first grief feels like being lost at sea i.e no connection to anything.</p>
<p>Then you get angry at someone, maybe a person who didn't attend the funeral, maybe a person who isn't around, maybe a person who is different now that your loved one has died. Suddenly you have a structure - - your anger toward them. The anger becomes a bridge over the open sea, a connection from you to them. It is something to hold onto and a connection made from the strength of anger feels better than nothing. We usually know more about suppressing anger than feeling it. The anger is just another indication of the intensity of your love.</p>
<p>c) Bargaining</p>
<p>Before a loss, it seems like you will do anything if only your loved one would be spared. "Please God," you bargain, "I will never be angry at my e.g wife again if you'll just let her live." After a loss, bargaining may take the form of a temporary truce. "What if I devote the rest of my life to helping others. Then can I wake up and realize this has all been a bad dream?" We become lost in a maze of "If only..." or "What if..." statements. We want life returned to what is was; we want our loved one restored.</p>
<p>We want to go back in time e.g find the tumor sooner, recognize the illness more quickly, stop the accident from happening...if only, if only, if only. Guilt is often bargaining's companion. The "if only" cause us to find fault in ourselves and what we "think" we could have done differently. We may even bargain with the pain. We will do anything not to feel the pain of this loss. We remain in the past, trying to negotiate our way out of the hurt. People often think of the stages as lasting weeks or months. They forget that the stages are responses to feelings that can last for minutes or hours as we flip in and out of one and then another. We do not enter and leave each individual stage in a linear fashion. We may feel one, then another and back again to the first one.</p>
<p>d) Depression</p>
<p>After bargaining, our attention moves squarely into the present. Empty feelings present themselves, and grief enters our lives on a deeper level, deeper than we ever imagined. This depressive stage feels as though it will last forever. It's important to understand that this depression is not a sign of mental illness. It is the appropriate response to a great loss. We withdraw from life, left in a fog of intense sadness, wondering, perhaps, if there is any point in going on alone? Why go on at all?</p>
<p>Depression after a loss is too often seen as unnatural, a state to be fixed, something to snap out of. The first question to ask yourself is whether or not the situation you're in is actually depressing. The loss of a loved one is a very depressing situation, and depression is a normal and appropriate response. To not experience depression after a loved one dies would be unusual. When a loss fully settles in your soul, the realization that your loved one didn't get better this time and is not coming back is understandably depressing. If grief is a process of healing, then depression is one of the many necessary steps along the way.</p>
<p>e) Acceptance</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Acceptance is often confused with the notion of being “all right” or “OK” with what has happened. This is not the case.</p>
<p>Most people don't ever feel OK or all right about the loss of a loved one. This stage is about accepting the reality that our loved one is physically gone and recognizing that this new reality is the permanent reality. We will never like this reality or make it OK, but eventually we accept it. We learn to live with it. It is the new norm that with which we must learn to live. We must try to live now in a world where our loved one is missing. In resisting this new norm, at first many people want to maintain life at it was before a love one died. In time, through bits and pieces of acceptance, however, we see that we cannot maintain the past intact. It has been forever changed and we must readjust. We must learn to reorganize roles, re-assign them to others or take them on ourselves.</p>
<p>Finding acceptance may be just having more good days than bad ones. As we begin to live again and enjoy our life, we often feel that in doing so, we are betraying our loved one. We can never replace what has been lost, but we can make new connections, new meaningful relationships, new inter-dependencies. Instead of denying our feelings, people should:</p>
<ul>
<li>listen to their needs</li>
<li>move on</li>
<li>change</li>
<li>grow</li>
<li>evolve</li>
</ul>
<p>We may start to reach out to others and become involved in their lives. We invest in our friendships and in our relationship with ourselves. We begin to live again, but we cannot do so until we have given grief its time.</p>
<p><strong>NB:</strong> In talking about phases or stages of grief, it is important to acknowledge the individuality of each grieving person and to be careful not to impose some pattern of grieving on others.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="breaking-bad-news">Breaking bad news<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#breaking-bad-news" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Nobody finds this easy. Telling someone that they have e.g. cancer or HIV, or that there is no cure for their illness, can be traumatic as we don't like causing pain. We may feel we won't know how to handle their reaction. However, the good news is that it is a skill we can learn. There will be differences in how it is done in different cultures, but here are some general guidelines, to remember them by the spelling <strong>BREAK NEWS</strong></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="be-prepared">Be prepared<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#be-prepared" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Make sure you have read or heard all the information that is available about the patient. Make sure you have enough time, do not start to break bad news when you only have a few minutes. Try to prevent interruptions, e.g. if you have a phone, turn it off.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="relatives">Relatives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#relatives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>It is usually best to give bad news when the patient has a close family member with them for support and to share the information most people only remember a small part of what is said when they are anxious or upset. It is important to check first who should be there to receive the news; this will vary according to the patient's culture and the setting. You might say: "We have some important things to discuss; are you happy for your mother to stay?" or "Who would you like to have with you while we talk?"</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="expectations">Expectations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#expectations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>What is the patient expecting to hear? Find out what they already know: "What have you been told about your illness? How do you think things are going?" This is very important even if it seems obvious to us, or even if we know they have already been told something by someone else. What has been said and what has been heard are not always the same. Listening to their thoughts will tell us what level of understanding they have, and what sort of language they use to talk about their illness.</p>
<p>Assess what is appropriate Try to assess how much they would like to know: "Have you been wondering what this is about? Would you like me to tell you what I think is going on?" It is not right to force information on someone who is not ready to hear it, just as it is not right to withhold information from someone who does want to know. We don't have to say everything in one visit – they may be ready to hear more next time.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="knowledge-sharing">Knowledge sharing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#knowledge-sharing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Share the knowledge you have slowly and gently, being careful to avoid words they won't understand. It is often helpful to let patients know you are about to tell them something important, you can give a warning such as: "We need to sit down and talk, things are quite serious". As you talk, give them time to absorb what you are saying and at the end, check they have understood.</p>
<p>People often want to know how much longer they will live. They may ask about this directly: "How long have I got left?" or indirectly: "When will I be able to go back to work?" It is usually unwise to give a precise answer because people are all individuals and often surprise us. However, it is helpful to give a rough idea to help people to plan realistically. Generally, if the patient's condition is deteriorating each month, they probably have some months left; if it is changing week by week, then it will be a matter of weeks; and if they are deteriorating every day, they may only have a few days left.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="never-say-there-is-nothing-we-can-do">Never say "there is nothing we can do"<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#never-say-there-is-nothing-we-can-do" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There is always something we can do and it is important to give positives as well as negatives: "We cannot cure the cancer, but we have medicine that will stop the pain." "We are always here to help if new problems arise".</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nb">NB:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nb" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Failure to break bad news lead to anxiety and complicating thing more</li>
<li>The breaking of bad news also need support and sharing of your feelings with colleague</li>
<li>Whenever bad news is explained completely and compassionately anxiolytics are usually rarely needed.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="care-after-death-last-offices">CARE AFTER DEATH / LAST OFFICES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#care-after-death-last-offices" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>This is the care given to a body after death</li>
<li>It demonstrates continued respect for the patient as an individual and in contemporary society is focused on fulfilling religious and cultural beliefs as well as health and safety and legal requirements (Lister and Dougherty, 2008).</li>
<li>Practices relating to care after death will vary depending on the patient’s religious and cultural background</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="factors-which-may-need-consideration-include">Factors which may need consideration include:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#factors-which-may-need-consideration-include" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Referral to e.g pathologist</li>
<li>Suspicious deaths</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; If a person has died in suspicious circumstances and a police investigation is likely, then the following procedure should be observed to preserve forensic evidence and minimize cross contamination:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>The body should not be washed or cleaned, unless express permission has been given by the senior police officer in charge of the investigation</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The body should not be touched by family and friends, unless express permission is given as above. The police will often allow supervised touching by the family.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>E.g A catholic priest should be permitted to anoint the forehead and administer the sacrament of the last rights to a dying person, or the recently deceased. It would be rare for the police to refuse permission for this</p>
</li>
<li>
