<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-chemotherapy">i) Chemotherapy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-chemotherapy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>This is the use of drugs</li>
</ul>
<p>Goal of chemotherapy are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Curative treatment are aimed at total elimination of cancerous cells, including those may spread to other parts of the body.</li>
<li>Adjuvant chemotherapy is used in addition to surgery and/or radiation to eliminate systemic microscopic cancerous cells. The purpose of adjuvant therapy is to provide a complete cure or remission.</li>
<li>The intent of palliative chemotherapy is to reduce tumor size, extend life and improve the overall quality of life.</li>
</ul>
<p>Chemotherapy agent can be classified in one of two ways:</p>
<ul>
<li>The effect the agent has on the cell</li>
<li>The pharmacologic properties of the agent</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ii-radiation-therapy">ii) Radiation therapy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-radiation-therapy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>This is the use of high-energy x-ray to treat cancer.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Radiation oncology is a subspecialty that incorporates the science and clinical application of therapeutic radiation in cancer therapy.</li>
</ul>
<p>Goals of radiation therapy:</p>
<ul>
<li>Treatment of cancer with ionizing radiation</li>
<li>Radiation destroys cells in the area being treated</li>
<li>Making it impossible for these cells to continue to grow.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="indications-for-radiation-therapy">Indications for radiation therapy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#indications-for-radiation-therapy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="cure">Cure:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Primary treatment for early stage of E.g Hodgkin's disease, cervical carcinoma</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="control">Control:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#control" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>To control the growth or spread of tumors/mass. Generally not a cure but may be curative and/or to allow less radical surgery</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Neo-adjuvant</strong> (administration of therapeutic agents before the main treatment E.g to enhance the outcome of the primary treatment):</p>
<ul>
<li>To “downstage” bulky tumors, such as large breast or rectal tumors. Intent may be curative and/or to allow less radical surgery</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="adjuvant">Adjuvant:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#adjuvant" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>To reduce the risk of local recurrence at the site of tumor such as breast radiation is given after lumpectomy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="palliation">Palliation:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#palliation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This is to optimize quality of life and mitigate suffering hence make patient feel comfortable. E.g Pain control for sites of bony metastasis, control of bulky metastasis to reduce morbidity such as massive lymphadenopathy</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="oncologic-emergencies">Oncologic emergencies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#oncologic-emergencies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Oncological emergencies are a group of conditions that occur as a direct or indirect result of cancer or its treatment that are potentially life-threatening</li>
<li>They are classified as <strong>metabolic, hematologic, structural, or treatment related</strong>.</li>
</ul>
<p>These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Hypercalcaemia</li>
<li>Neutropaenic sepsis</li>
<li>Tumour lysis syndrome</li>
<li>Leukostasis</li>
<li>Raised intracranial pressure</li>
<li>Spinal cord compression</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Cauda equina syndrome</li>
<li>Superior vena cava obstruction</li>
<li>Syndrome of inappropriate antidiuretic hormone secretion (SIADH)</li>
<li>Disseminated intravascular coagulation</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="iii-surgery">iii) Surgery<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-surgery" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>This is the removal of the tumor, the surrounding tissue and lymph nodes.</li>
<li>It can be conventional instruments or laser.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="some-other-modern-modalities">Some other modern modalities:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#some-other-modern-modalities" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="i-hormonal-therapy">i) Hormonal therapy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-hormonal-therapy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>Use of hormones to change the way hormones in the body help cancer to grow.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="ii-biological-therapy">ii) Biological therapy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-biological-therapy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>Immunotherapy- makes use of the body’s immune system.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="iii-stem-cell-therapies-etc">iii) Stem cell therapies etc<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-stem-cell-therapies-etc" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="main-cancer-groups">Main cancer groups<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#main-cancer-groups" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Cancers are divided into groups according to the type of cell they start from. They include</p>
<ul>
<li>Carcinomas</li>
<li>Lymphomas</li>
<li>Leukaemias</li>
<li>Brain tumours</li>
<li>Sarcomas</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="classification-of-cancer-depending-on-the-cell-tissue-of-origin">Classification of cancer depending on the cell/tissue of origin<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification-of-cancer-depending-on-the-cell-tissue-of-origin" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>epithelial tissues (carcinoma)</li>
<li>glandular tissues (adenocarcinomas)</li>
<li>connective, muscle and bone tissues (sarcomas)</li>
<li>brain and spinal cord tissues (gliomas)</li>
<li>pigmented cells (melanomas)</li>
<li>plasma cells (myelomas)</li>
<li>lymphatic tissue (lymphomas)</li>
<li>leukocytes (leukemia)</li>
<li>erythrocytes (erythroleukemia)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="prevention-and-detection-of-cancer">Prevention and detection of cancer<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-and-detection-of-cancer" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Promoting risk factors awareness</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Promoting healthy behaviors</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Limiting alcohol consumption</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Hepatitis B virus infant vaccination</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Control of STDs</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Changing risk behaviors</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Teaching skills for early detection programs</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Promoting participation in early detection programs</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Maintain a healthy weight throughout life.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Balance caloric intake with physical activity</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Avoid excessive weight gain throughout the life cycle</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Achieve and maintain a healthy weight if currently overweight or obese</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Adopt a physically active lifestyle.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Adults: engage in at least 30 minutes of moderate to vigorous physical activity, above usual activities, on 5 or more days of the week; 45 to 60 minutes of intentional physical activity are preferable</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Children and adolescents: engage in at least 60 minutes per day of moderate to vigorous physical activity at least 5 days per week</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Consume a healthy diet, with an emphasis on plant sources</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Choose foods and beverages in amounts that help achieve and maintain a healthy weight</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Eat five or more servings of a variety of vegetables and fruits each day</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Limit consumption of processed and red meats</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If you drink alcoholic beverages, limit consumption.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="basic-principles-of-nutrition-in-patients-with-cancer">Basic principles of nutrition in patients with cancer<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#basic-principles-of-nutrition-in-patients-with-cancer" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The nutrition status of patients with cancer can vary at presentation and through the continuum of cancer care. Many patients experience unintentional weight loss leading to a diagnosis of cancer. Studies have reported malnutrition in 30% to 85% of patients with cancer. Historically, weight loss, low body mass index (BMI) and serum albumin levels have been used as surrogate markers for malnutrition.</p>
<p>Emerging evidence supports that loss of lean body mass (sarcopenia) in patients with cancer is an independent risk factor for poorer outcomes and that in the setting of obesity, unlike in other diseases where weight loss may be welcomed, inappropriate loss of weight may lead to loss of muscle mass and poorer outcomes. However, there is no universal definition of sarcopenia, and there are no simple methods to identify the condition, limiting application in clinical practice.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="malnutrition">Malnutrition<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#malnutrition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In 2010, the American Society for Parenteral and Enteral Nutrition (ASPEN) and the European Society for Clinical Nutrition and Metabolism published their proposed etiology-based definitions of malnutrition. These have been accepted by both groups and the Academy of Nutrition and Dietetics.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The consensus statement provides the criteria for evaluating each of the following six potential indicators of malnutrition, with the recommendation that if two or more characteristics are present, the diagnosis of malnutrition is warranted and these are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Insufficient energy intake</li>
<li>Weight loss</li>
<li>Loss of muscle mass</li>
<li>Loss of subcutaneous fat</li>
<li>Localized or generalized fluid accumulation that may sometimes mask weight loss</li>
<li>Diminished functional status as measured by hand grip strength</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nutrition-support">Nutrition support<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nutrition-support" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Nutrition support is the delivery of nutrition that bypasses oral intake. Every measure is employed to sustain patients and improve their condition through oral intake before consideration is given to nutrition support. Enteral nutrition (tube feeding) provides nutrition directly into the GI tract. Parenteral nutrition is the intravenous (IV) infusion of nutrients.</p>
<p>The use of enteral and parenteral nutrition in the oncology population may be indicated when oral nutrition strategies are not possible or fail because of tumor location or severe side effects. Although nutrition support is not recommended as standard treatment, it may be beneficial for patients who are malnourished and expected to become unable to take in adequate nutrition by mouth for an extended period of time. There are concerns that use of nutrition support will stimulate tumor growth and metastasis, but studies in humans are limited and show mixed results. However, if nutrition support is clinically indicated, it should not be withheld because of concerns about tumor promotion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="why-enteral-nutrition-is-preferred-over-parenteral-nutrition-in-most-instances">Why Enteral nutrition is preferred over parenteral nutrition in most instances<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#why-enteral-nutrition-is-preferred-over-parenteral-nutrition-in-most-instances" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Enteral nutrition continues to use the gut</li>
<li>is associated with fewer infectious complications</li>
<li>is often easier to administer</li>
<li>is more cost-effective than parenteral nutrition</li>
</ul>
<p>Indications for nutrition support include the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Patient is moderately to severely malnourished, will undergo major surgery and is anticipated to not achieve adequate oral nutrition for at least 7 to 14 days post-surgery.</li>
<li>Patient is malnourished and anticipated to have inadequate ingestion or absorption for 7 to 14 days or longer.</li>
<li>Patient has a mechanical obstruction preventing food from reaching the small bowel for proper digestion and absorption.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Providing nutrition support routinely to patients undergoing chemotherapy or radiation therapy is not recommended rather, nutrition support is reserved for patients who meet any of the criteria listed above. It is sometimes difficult to know which patients will have a prolonged period of inadequate oral intake or malabsorption and will benefit from nutrition support. For patients undergoing head and neck radiation, investigators have validated an evidenced-based protocol for determining which patients are at high risk of nutrition deficiency and therefore proactive placement of a gastrostomy tube</p>
<p>Although aggressive nutrition support has been shown to improve quality of life in patients with advanced cancer, it is generally not recommended if life expectancy is shorter than 40 to 60 days. For some patients who have incurable disease and are undergoing anticancer treatment, such as those with bowel obstruction, nutrition support may be appropriate. Investigators have suggested the following additional criteria for withholding nutrition support in patients with advanced disease:</p>
<ul>
<li>Short estimated life expectancy (fewer than 2–3 months)</li>
<li>Poor performance status as determined by a Karnofsky Performance Status score lower than 50% or an Eastern Cooperative Oncology Group Performance Status grade of 3 or 4</li>
<li>Severe organ dysfunction</li>
<li>Uncontrolled symptoms</li>
<li>Patient choice</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="initiating-nutrition-support">Initiating nutrition support<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#initiating-nutrition-support" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Despite established guidelines on optimal management of nutrition in patients with cancer, a wide range of opinions and practices exist among cancer practitioners. Lack of awareness among health professionals of the need for nutrition support in cancer patients has also been reported. Unfortunately, these gaps in care have led to reports of patients failing to receive adequate nutrition when appropriate.</li>
<li>Although the decision to initiate nutrition support in severely malnourished patients is simple, it becomes more complex in patients without a defined period of time for nutrition requirements, in patients with incurable disease, for immunosuppressed patients and for those who are borderline malnourished.</li>
<li>All patients with cancer should undergo nutritional screening throughout their treatment courses. Early screening provides a baseline value to identify nutritional needs over time. The scoring tools mentioned earlier, which have been validated in the oncology setting, can aid in such screening.</li>
<li>Counseling regarding adequate dietary intake and supplementation should be provided to all patients with adequate oral intake who are malnourished or at risk for poor nutrition.</li>
<li>Nutrition support should be initiated in patients with inadequate intake (&lt;60% of caloric requirements) for an expected duration not less than 7 days, and malnourished patients should be considered for nutrition support 7 days prior to surgery, as this may improve postoperative outcomes</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nutrition-counseling">Nutrition counseling<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nutrition-counseling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Initiate nutrition counseling as soon as such needs are identified, as there is strong evidence that nutrition counseling in patients with cancer can prevent and reduce malnutrition. Counseling should be provided</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>by nutritional support personnel with expertise in appropriate dietary intervention and monitoring in cancer care.</p>
<p>Nutritional plans should be individualized to meet the needs of the patient, taking into consideration their nutrition status, type and stage of disease, comorbid conditions, and overall medical treatment plan. One study in patients with head and neck cancer undergoing radiation demonstrated that nutritional counseling lead to improvement in anorexia, xerostomia (gry mouth), and dysgeusia. Quality-of-life outcomes were also improved in patients who received nutritional counselling</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="methods-of-nutrition-therapy">Methods of nutrition therapy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#methods-of-nutrition-therapy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Prompt and aggressive nutrition intervention is required for patients with pre-cachexia or cancer cachexia. Intervention is more likely to be effective when started early. Interventions include an individualized approach to oral, enteral and parenteral nutrition using evidence-based recommendations, guidelines and program and regulatory standards.</p>
<p>The dietician works with the patient, caregivers and members of the health care team to improve compliance and the effectiveness of pharmacotherapy interventions prescribed to manage cancer and cancer treatment-related symptoms and counsel patients about behavioral strategies to alleviate nutrition impact symptoms</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-enteral-route-and-administration">i) Enteral route and administration<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-enteral-route-and-administration" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Several effective methods for the delivery of enteral nutrition exist. Factors affecting a choice of the enteral route include:</p>
<ul>
<li>anticipated length of need,</li>
<li>aspiration risk, tumor location</li>
<li>side effects.</li>
</ul>
<p>Assessment of need is best performed early. If a malnourished patient requires surgery for an unrelated event, a feeding tube may be placed at that time to avoid an additional procedure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="short-term-feeding">Short-term feeding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#short-term-feeding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>For short-term feeding (&lt;2 weeks), a naso-enteric tube may be best. The risk of aspiration is considered in the determination of the proper termination point of the tube; stomach (nasogastric tube), duodenum (naso-duodenal tube), or jejunum (naso-jejunal tube), with nasojejunal feeds recommended for patients with aspiration risk.</p>
<p>Tubes are constructed of silicone or polyurethane and can vary in length from 30 to 43 inches, with the shorter tubes used for nasogastric feedings. Diameters range from 5F catheters to 16F catheters. Tubes may have weighted tips to help passage through the gut. If a patient with cancer is at very high risk of aspiration, enteral nutrition may be contraindicated, and parenteral nutrition can be considered. Immuno-compromised patients with mucositis, esophagitis, and/or herpetic, fungal, or candidiasis lesions in the mouth or throat may not be able to tolerate the presence of a naso-enteric tube.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="longer-term-feeding">Longer-term feeding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#longer-term-feeding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>For longer-term feeding (&gt;4 weeks), direct enteral access is recommended. Percutaneous tubes may be placed endoscopically, surgically, or with fluoroscopy by interventional radiology.</p>
<p>Percutaneous tube placement has a number of advantages:</p>
<ul>
<li>the diameter of the tube may be larger (15F–24F catheters) allowing easier and faster passage of formulas and medications</li>
<li>the risk of aspiration is lower because of the decreased chance of migration of the tube into the esophagus;</li>
<li>the risk of sinusitis or naso-esophageal erosion is lower and this route is more convenient and aesthetically pleasing to patients because of their ability to conceal the tube.</li>
</ul>
<p>Conversion to a skin-level button gastrostomy or jejunostomy may also be considered when longer-term support is anticipated.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="infusion-methods-and-formulas">Infusion methods and formulas<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#infusion-methods-and-formulas" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Administration methods vary depending on where in the GI tract the tube terminates and may be affected by treatment side effects.</p>
<p>For tubes terminating in the stomach, a bolus or intermittent (gravity) drip may be possible and is preferable because it mimics normal feeding, requires less time and equipment and offers greater flexibility to the patient. For tubes terminating in the duodenum or jejunum, an infusion pump is required because a slower administration rate is necessary. Feedings via a pump may be administered cyclically (&lt;24 hours per day) or continuously</p>
<p>The following lists summarize infusion methods and considerations for initiation and administration of enteral nutrition:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-bolus-and-intermittent-feeding">a) Bolus and intermittent feeding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-bolus-and-intermittent-feeding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Caloric/nutrient and free-water requirements need to be determined to plan the feeding schedule.</li>
<li>Bolus feedings can be offered several times (3–6 times) each day, as much as 250 to 500 cc can be given over 10 to 15 minutes.</li>
<li>Bolus feeding should be used only when the endpoint of the tube is in the stomach, it should never be used when feedings are delivered into the duodenum or jejunum. This precaution protects against gastric distension and dumping.</li>
<li>A gravity drip from a bag or syringe with a slow push can be used to administer the formula.</li>
<li>Diarrhea is a common side effect of this infusion method but can be controlled with a change in formula, additions to the formula, and a change in the amount of formula given over a finite period of time.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="b-continuous-or-cyclic-drip-feeding">b) Continuous or cyclic drip feeding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-continuous-or-cyclic-drip-feeding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Caloric/nutrient and free-water requirements need to be determined first to plan rate and time recommendations.</li>
<li>Enteral feeding pumps provide reliable, constant infusion rates and decrease the risk of gastric retention.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>When no compounding factors are present, feeding into the stomach (25–30 cc/h) can start at a higher rate than feeding into the jejunum (10 cc/h); rates can be increased, with tolerance, every 4 to 6 hours until the rate reaches that needed to deliver the required caloric/nutrient needs.</li>
<li>Continuous feeds can be cycled to run at night to allow greater flexibility and comfort. If it is physically possible, these nocturnal feeds can allow daytime oral or bolus feedings to meet nutrition goals and provide a more normal lifestyle.</li>
</ul>
<p>Enteral formulas vary in nutrient composition and source. Most commercially available formulas are lactose free, kosher, and halal. Standard/polymeric formulations are appropriate for most patients. Semi-elemental and elemental formulas are available for patients with mal-absorption who do not or will not tolerate standard formulas. In some cases, disease-specific (renal, pulmonary, and diabetic) formulas may be appropriate and are available but in general are not necessary unless the patient has a demonstrated "failure" with standard formulas.</p>
<p>The use of whole-food blenderized formulas is gaining in popularity. Some products are commercially available and there are published recipes for home-made formula. It is important for a dietician to thoroughly review the nutrient content of these home-blenderized formulas to ensure adequacy.</p>
<p>For patients in the perioperative setting, evidence supports the use of IE formulas. The most widely studied formula in this category contains a combination of arginine, omega-3 fatty acids, and nucleotides. Studies suggest that use of these formulas for a very narrow period of time can reduce the incidence of surgical complications (infectious and non-infectious) and decrease the length of hospital stays</p>
<p>ii) Parenteral route and administration</p>
<p>&gt; If parenteral nutrition is determined to be beneficial and appropriate, it can be administered via central or peripheral venous access. Many patients with cancer already have central IV catheters to accommodate multiple IV therapies. For patients who do not already have central line access or will not have it for a period of time, a peripheral catheter can be placed, however, care must be taken to avoid overuse of the peripheral IVs, as this can result in vessel sclerosis. To minimize venous complications, the use of peripheral parenteral nutrition is limited</p>
<p>&gt; Parenteral nutrition is a combination of:
&gt; - dextrose (carbohydrate)
&gt; - amino acids (protein)
&gt; - lipid emulsions (fat)
&gt; - added electrolytes
&gt; - vitamins
&gt; - trace elements.</p>
<p>It is recommended that parenteral nutrition management include clinicians with expertise in nutrition support and be made up of a multidisciplinary team, including a registered dietician and clinical pharmacist</p>
<p>&gt; Parenteral nutrition is typically initiated as a 24-hour infusion. After tolerance is established and generally after daily macronutrient goals are achieved, parenteral nutrition may be cycled (typically to an</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>infusion time of 10–14 hours). For patients who will be receiving home parenteral nutrition, a cyclic infusion is preferred</p>
<p>&gt; It is generally recommended that parenteral nutrition be initiated in the hospital and not at home. Only if the benefits of home initiation far outweigh the risks should it be considered, and only for patients who are hemo-dynamically stable, at low risk of re-feeding syndrome and non-diabetic</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="impediments-to-adequate-nutrition">Impediments to adequate nutrition<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#impediments-to-adequate-nutrition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Influences on nutrition status and risk of malnutrition include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Baseline nutrition status</li>
<li>Disease site</li>
<li>Stage of disease</li>
<li>Treatment approach</li>
</ul>
<p>Treatment approaches, including surgery, chemotherapy and radiation therapy, can have a direct (mechanical) negative effect and/or an indirect (metabolic) negative effect on nutrition status. The success of anticancer therapy is affected by the patient's nutrition status before and during treatment, which influences the patient's ability to tolerate therapy.</p>
<p>Oral intake is impeded by nutrition impact symptoms, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>anorexia</li>
<li>alterations in taste and smell</li>
<li>mucositis</li>
<li>dysphagia</li>
<li>stomatitis</li>
<li>nausea</li>
<li>vomiting</li>
<li>diarrhea</li>
<li>constipation</li>
<li>malabsorption</li>
<li>pain</li>
<li>depression and anxiety</li>
</ul>
<p>Pre-existing comorbidities may also play a role in the development of cancer, e.g., alcohol abuse and obesity or may increase the risk of malnutrition at presentation (Bozzetti F, et al, 2012)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tumor-induced-effects-on-nutrition-status">Tumor-induced effects on nutrition status<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tumor-induced-effects-on-nutrition-status" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Tumors may have systemic or local effects that affect nutrition status, including hyper-metabolism, malabsorption, dysmotility and obstructions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tumor-metabolism">Tumor metabolism<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tumor-metabolism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Nutrition status can be compromised in direct response to tumor-induced alterations in metabolism (i.e., cachexia). Tumor-induced weight loss occurs frequently in patients with solid tumors of the lung, pancreas, and upper gastrointestinal (GI) tract and less often in patients with breast cancer or lower Gastro-intestinal cancer. Cachexia is also more common with more-advanced disease (Baldwin C, et al, 2012)</p>
<p>In 2011, an international group of experts developed a consensus definition of cachexia as "a multifactorial syndrome defined by an on-going loss of skeletal muscle mass that cannot be fully reversed by conventional nutrition support and leads to progressive functional impairment." The classified three stages of cachexia:</p>
<ul>
<li>Precachexia: early signs (clinical and metabolic) that precede substantial weight loss.</li>
<li>Cachexia: the presence of significant weight loss or sarcopenia in the absence of simple starvation.</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; Weight loss &gt;5% over the past 6 months or
&gt; Body mass index &lt;20 and degree of weight loss &gt;2% or
&gt; Sarcopenia and any degree of weight loss &gt;2%.</p>
<ul>
<li>Refractory cachexia: cachexia that is clinically refractory is usually associated with advanced-stage cancer or rapid progression of disease that is unresponsive to treatment.</li>
</ul>
<p>Although anorexia may also be present, the energy deficit alone does not explain the pathogenesis of cachexia. The etiology of cancer cachexia is not entirely understood, but several factors have been proposed including:</p>
<p>&gt; cytokines,
&gt; neuropeptides,
&gt; neurotransmitters,
&gt; tumor-derived factors. All these are postulated to contribute to the syndrome products of host tissues (e.g., tumor necrosis factor-alpha) and have a direct catabolic effect on host tissues.</p>
<p>Altered metabolism of fats, proteins and carbohydrates is evident in patients with cancer cachexia. Tumors may impair glucose uptake and glucose oxidation, leading to an increased glycolysis.</p>
<p>Weight loss can occur from a decrease in energy intake and/or an increase in energy expenditure. Although anorexia is a common symptom of patients with cancer, studies have shown that increased caloric intake, whether by the oral route or by supplementation with total parenteral nutrition, has failed to counteract the wasting process. This aberrant metabolic rate appears to be a direct response by the tumor and immune system to disrupt the pathways that regulate the body-weight regulation homeostasis loop.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-induced-effects-on-nutrition-status">Treatment-induced effects on nutrition status<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-induced-effects-on-nutrition-status" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Cancer treatments may cause acute and chronic effects. Nutrition intervention is based on symptom management. Patients who maintain good nutrition are more likely to tolerate the side effects of treatment. Adequate calories and protein can help maintain patient strength and prevent body tissues from further catabolism. Side effects of cancer treatments vary among patients, depending on:</p>
<ul>
<li>the type of treatment</li>
<li>length of treatment</li>
<li>dose of treatments</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>the type of cancer being treated</li>
</ul>
<p>Cancer treatment has toxic effects on the GI tract, including nausea, vomiting, constipation, diarrhea, xerostomia, mucositis, dysphagia and loss of appetite.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="chemotherapy-and-hormone-therapy">Chemotherapy and hormone therapy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chemotherapy-and-hormone-therapy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Chemotherapy and hormone therapy can be used as single agents or in combination, depending on the disease type and patient's health condition. These agents are divided into several functional categories. For instance, chemotherapy is a systemic treatment (not a localized treatment) that affects the whole body (not just a specific part) and potentially causes more side effects when compared with localized treatments such as surgery and radiation therapy.</p>
<p>Common nutrition-related side effects include:</p>
<ul>
<li>anorexia</li>
<li>taste changes</li>
<li>early satiety</li>
<li>nausea</li>
<li>vomiting</li>
<li>mucositis/esophagitis</li>
<li>diarrhea</li>
<li>constipation</li>
</ul>
<p>Cancer and the side effects of chemotherapy can greatly affect nutrition status, so health care providers must anticipate possible problems and formulate a plan with the patient to prevent malnutrition and weight loss. Malnutrition and weight loss can affect a patient's ability to regain health and acceptable blood counts between chemotherapy cycles and this can directly affect the patient's ability to stay on treatment schedules, which is important in achieving a successful outcome.</p>
<p>Patients receiving hormone suppression therapies are at risk of weight gain rather than weight loss. These patients may benefit from directed education to minimize weight gain and help reduce the risk of developing comorbidities associated with excess body weight.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="radiation-therapy">Radiation therapy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#radiation-therapy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Radiation therapy causes localized symptoms. Some of the common nutrition-related side effects caused by irradiation include:</p>
<ul>
<li>changes in taste</li>
<li>ability to swallow,</li>
<li>nausea/vomiting,</li>
<li>changes in bowel movements (usually diarrhea),</li>
<li>GI symptoms such as gas.</li>
</ul>
<p>The side effects of radiation therapy depend on:</p>
<ul>
<li>the area that is irradiated</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>total dose</li>
<li>fractionation</li>
<li>duration</li>
<li>volume irradiated</li>
</ul>
<p>Most side effects are acute, begin around the second or third week of treatment and diminish 2 or 3 weeks after radiation therapy is completed. Some side effects can be chronic and continue or occur after treatment has been completed.</p>
<p>Nutrition support during radiation therapy is vital. The effect of radiation therapy on healthy tissue in the treatment field can produce changes in normal physiologic function that may ultimately diminish a patient's nutrition status by interfering with ingestion, digestion,</p>
<p>or absorption of nutrients.</p>
<p>Many nutrition-related side effects result from radiation therapy. Quality of life and nutrition intake can be improved by managing these side effects through appropriate medical nutrition therapy and dietary modifications. For example, medications such as pilocarpine (Salagen) may be useful in treating the xerostomia that accompanies radiation therapy targeting the head and neck. This medicine may reduce the need for artificial saliva agents or other oral comfort agents such as hard candy or sugarless gum.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="surgery">Surgery<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#surgery" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>For patients with most types of solid tumors, surgery is the only chance for a cure. Although a tumor may be technically resectable, a meaningful recovery can depend on a patient's preoperative nutrition status. Patients who are malnourished at the time of surgery are at higher risk of postoperative morbidity and mortality and longer hospital stays. If time permits or if the surgical procedure may be delayed safely, steps can be taken to identify patients who are moderately to severely malnourished before surgery and to correct macronutrient and micronutrient deficiencies before surgery. Choosing the best method to correct a nutrition deficiency depends on GI tract function; options include oral liquid nutrition supplements, and enteral or parenteral nutrition support.</p>
<p>Surgical treatment can increase occurrence of malnutrition or worsen malnutrition. Common side effects of surgery, especially to the GI tract or head and neck, include:</p>
<ul>
<li>decreased appetite,</li>
<li>decreased ability to take food by mouth,</li>
<li>early satiety, all of which can lead to worsening pre-existing malnutrition or may cause previously adequately nourished patients to become malnourished after surgery</li>
</ul>
<p>Depending on the procedure, surgery can cause mechanical or physiologic barriers to adequate nutrition, such as a short gut that results in mal-absorption after bowel resection. In addition to these mechanical barriers, surgery frequently leads to an immediate catabolic response and changes the nutrient requirements necessary for wound healing and recovery at a time when baseline needs and requirements are often not being met.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="biotherapy">Biotherapy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#biotherapy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Bicameral, Bicameral, and Bicameral</p>
<p>Biotherapy is treatment to boost the immune system to help enhance the body's own response against cancer or to help repair normal cells damaged as a side effect of treatment. Biotherapy includes growth factors, monoclonal antibodies, and vaccines. The symptoms of biotherapy that are most likely to impact nutrition status are:</p>
<ul>
<li>fatigue,</li>
<li>fever,</li>
<li>nausea,</li>
<li>vomiting,</li>
<li>diarrhea.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hemopoietic-cell-transplantation-hct">Hemopoietic cell transplantation (HCT)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hemopoietic-cell-transplantation-hct" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>HCT patients can have special nutrition requirements. Before cell transplant, patients receive high-dose chemotherapy and may be treated with total-body irradiation. In addition to the medications used during transplantation, these treatments frequently result in nutrition-related side effects, including mucositis and significant diarrhea, which may affect the ability of patients to consume an adequate diet. Patients may also experience acute or chronic graft-versus-host disease (GVHD). GVHD may target the GI tract, liver, or skin, altering the body's ability to ingest and process adequate calories and protein</p>
<p>The goal of nutrition support is to maintain adequate nutrition status and protein stores. The American Society for Parenteral and Enteral Nutrition recommends that patients undergoing HCT who are malnourished and anticipated to be unable to ingest or absorb adequate nutrients for a prolonged period of time (&gt;7–14 days) receive nutrition support; if a patient has a functioning GI tract, enteral nutrition is recommended.</p>
<p>In addition, transplant patients are at very high risk of neutropenia, an abnormally small number of neutrophils in the blood that increases susceptibility to multiple infections. To reduce the risk of infections related to HCT, patients can receive dietary counseling regarding safe food handling and avoidance of foods that may pose an infection risk.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nutrition-screening-and-assessment">Nutrition screening and assessment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nutrition-screening-and-assessment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Optimizing nutrition for patients with cancer involves early detection of malnutrition or risk of malnutrition so that intervention may be initiated in the early stages of disease or treatment. The goal of nutrition screening is to rapidly identify patients who are at risk of developing malnutrition and refer them to a health care professional, ideally a registered dietician, who can perform a complete nutrition assessment and implement a nutrition care plan (Levin R, et al, 2018)</p>
<p>There are no standard definitions or indices of malnutrition. Historically, loss of weight or body mass index (BMI), low BMI, and low serum protein (e.g., albumin) have been used to identify patients with malnutrition. Without more context, these characteristics are not acceptable measures by which to determine malnutrition. Weight changes alone cannot be used to determine nutrition status because weight changes do not account for fluid changes (dehydration, ascites, and edema) or disproportionate loss of lean body mass. Likewise, evidence demonstrates that BMI is deceiving because it does not account for body composition (lean body mass vs. fat mass), and many patients with cancer may present with a normal or high body weight/BMI but have severe</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>muscle depletion (i.e., sarcopenia) The use of albumin, which is now recognized as being significantly influenced by inflammation, is also a poor measure of nutrition status and more likely suggestive of disease severity, not nutrition status. Standardized definitions and cut-off points that designate malnutrition or cachexia are being developed, however, the true prevalence of malnutrition in the oncology population is unknown (August DA et al, 2014)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="assessment">Assessment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#assessment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Nutrition assessment is a comprehensive approach to evaluating and diagnosing nutrition problems and designing interventions. A full nutrition assessment involves evaluation of six components:</p>
<ul>
<li>Food- and nutrition-related history</li>
<li>Anthropometric measurements.</li>
<li>Biochemical data, medical tests, and procedures.</li>
<li>Nutrition-focused physical assessment.</li>
<li>Medical history.</li>
<li>Treatment plan.</li>
</ul>
<p>Within the nutrition assessment, the following factors are considered in making a diagnosis of malnutrition</p>
<ul>
<li>Insufficient energy intake</li>
<li>Weight loss</li>
<li>Loss of muscle mass</li>
<li>Loss of subcutaneous fat</li>
<li>Localized or generalized fluid accumulation</li>
<li>Diminished functional status (e.g., grip strength).</li>
</ul>
<p>The assessment of anthropometric measurements evaluates weight loss, takes into account the time frame of weight loss and is considered in the context of physical findings such as dehydration or fluid retention. Evaluation of food- and nutrition-related history ideally involves a dietician obtaining a diet history and comparing intake with the patient's calculated energy needs. The nutrition-focused physical assessment evaluates loss of muscle mass and subcutaneous fat, fluid accumulation and potential micronutrient deficiencies. The physical examination of the following areas determines loss of subcutaneous fat or muscle:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="subcutaneous-fat-loss">Subcutaneous fat loss<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#subcutaneous-fat-loss" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Orbit.</li>
<li>Upper arm</li>
<li>Thoracic and lumbar regions</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="subcutaneous-muscle-loss">Subcutaneous muscle loss<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#subcutaneous-muscle-loss" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Temple.</li>
<li>Clavicle.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Clavicle and acromion.</li>
<li>Scapula.</li>
<li>Dorsal hand.</li>
<li>Patella.</li>
<li>Anterior thigh.</li>
<li>Posterior calf.</li>
</ul>
<p>In addition to the issues described above, the oncology nutrition assessment also takes into account the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Tumor location (current or anticipated mechanical function impairment).</li>
<li>Current side effects/symptoms.</li>
<li>Anticipated treatment/side effects.</li>
<li>Anticipated duration of symptoms.</li>
<li>Intent of treatment.</li>
</ul>
<p>The goal of an oncology nutrition assessment is to collect the information necessary to determine current or anticipated nutrition issues and to formulate a plan with the patient, caregivers and other members of the health care team involved with nutrition interventions. Additionally, this multidisciplinary team approach may also include metabolic, pharmacologic and functional interventions to address and prevent the identified or anticipated nutrition issues (Levin R., et al, 2018)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="behavioral-strategies-for-symptom-management">Behavioral strategies for symptom management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#behavioral-strategies-for-symptom-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Cancer and cancer treatment result in a range of side effects, described as nutrition impact symptoms, that impede oral intake. While some patients experience few of these effects, others may have multiple symptoms, including anorexia, early satiety, constipation, diarrhea, dysphagia, fatigue, mucositis, nausea, taste and smell changes, and xerostomia. These symptoms can result in a decline in nutrition status and quality of life. Behavioral strategies are essential in alleviating the impact of these symptoms and promoting adequate nutrient intake; pharmacologic interventions may be used in combination with these strategies to minimize symptom severity.</p>
<p>The following lists describe behavioral strategies to help alleviate nutrition-related symptoms of cancer treatment..</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="behavioral-strategies-to-alleviate-nutrition-related-symptoms">Behavioral strategies to alleviate nutrition-related symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#behavioral-strategies-to-alleviate-nutrition-related-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Loss of Appetite and Weight Loss</li>
<li>Eat small, frequent meals and healthy snacks throughout the day.</li>
<li>Eat foods that are high in protein and calories.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>&gt; Eat high-protein foods first in your meal while your appetite is strongest, foods such as beans, chicken, fish, meat, yogurt and eggs.
&gt; Add extra protein and calories to food.
&gt; Cook with protein-fortified milk.
&gt; Drink milkshakes, smoothies, juices, or soups if you do not feel like eating solid foods.
&gt; Prepare and store small portions of favorite foods.
&gt; Seek foods that appeal to the sense of smell.
&gt; Experiment with different foods.
&gt; Eat larger meals when you feel well and are rested.
&gt; Sip only small amounts of liquids during meals.
&gt; Eat your largest meal when you feel hungriest, whether at breakfast, lunch, or dinner.
&gt; Be as active as possible to help develop a bigger appetite.
&gt; Consider asking your health practitioner about blenderized drinks with a high nutrient density.
&gt; Tell your doctor if you are having eating problems such as nausea, vomiting, or changes in how foods taste and smell.
&gt; Perform frequent mouth care to relieve symptoms and decrease aftertastes.
&gt; Consider tube feedings if you are unable to sustain a certain amount of caloric intake to maintain strength.</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Constipation
&gt; Drink plenty of fluids each day, including water, warm juices and prune juice.
&gt; Be active each day; take walks regularly.
&gt; Eat more fiber-containing foods.
&gt; Drink hot liquids to help relieve constipation, including coffee, tea and warm milk.
&gt; Talk with your doctor before taking laxatives, stool softeners, or any medicine to relieve constipation.
&gt; Limit certain foods if you develop gas, including broccoli, cabbage, cauliflower, beans and cucumbers.
&gt; Eat a large breakfast, including a hot drink and high-fiber foods.
&gt; Consider a fiber supplement.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Diarrhea
&gt; Drink plenty of fluids to replace those lost from diarrhea, including water, ginger ale and sports drinks.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>&gt; Let carbonated drinks lose their fizz before you drink them.
&gt; Eat foods and liquids that are high in sodium and potassium.
&gt; - Liquids: E.g bouillon or fat-free broth.
&gt; - Foods: E.g bananas; canned apricots; and baked, boiled, or mashed potatoes.
&gt; Eat low-fiber foods.
&gt; Have foods and drinks at room temperature (neither too hot nor too cold).
&gt; Avoid foods such as:
&gt; - High-fiber foods.
&gt; - High-sugar foods.
&gt; - Very hot or cold drinks.
&gt; - Greasy, fatty and fried foods.
&gt; - Foods that can cause gas, such as carbonated beverages, cruciferous vegetables, legumes and lentils and chewing gum.
&gt; - Milk products (unless low lactose or lactose free).
&gt; - Alcohol.
&gt; - Spicy foods.
&gt; - Caffeinated drinks.
&gt; - Sugar-free products sweetened with xylitol or sorbitol.</p>
<ul>
<li>Dry Mouth
&gt; - Sip water throughout the day.
&gt; Have very sweet or tart foods and drinks – such as lemonade, to help make more saliva.
&gt; - Chew gum or suck on hard candy, ice pops, or ice chips; sugar free is best, but consult your doctor if you also have diarrhea.
&gt; Eat foods that are easy to swallow.
&gt; - Moisten food with sauce, gravy, or salad dressing.
&gt; - Do not drink any type of alcohol, beer, or wine.
&gt; - Avoid foods that can hurt your mouth, i.e., spicy, sour, salty, hard, or crunchy foods.
&gt; - Keep your lips moist with lip balm.
&gt; - Rinse your mouth every 1 to 2 hours.
&gt; - Do not use mouthwash that contains alcohol.
&gt; - Do not use tobacco products, and avoid second-hand smoke.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>&gt; Talk with your doctor or dentist about artificial saliva or other products to coat, protect and moisten your throat and mouth.</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Lactose intolerance
&gt; Prepare your own low-lactose or lactose-free foods.
&gt; Choose lactose-free or low-lactose milk products. Most grocery stores carry products (such as milk and ice cream) labelled "lactose free" or "low lactose."
&gt; Try products made with soy or rice (such as soy or rice milk and frozen desserts). These products do not contain any lactose.
&gt; Choose milk products that are low in lactose. Hard cheeses (such as cheddar) and yogurt are less likely to cause problems.
&gt; Try using lactase tablets when consuming dairy products. Lactase is an enzyme that breaks down lactose.
&gt; Avoid only the milk products that give you problems. Try small portions of milk, yogurt, or cheese to see if you can tolerate them.
&gt; Try calcium-fortified non-dairy drinks and foods, which you can identify by food labels.
&gt; Eat more calcium-rich vegetables, including broccoli and greens.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Nausea
&gt; Eat bland, soft, easy-to-digest foods rather than heavy meals.
&gt; Eat dry foods such as crackers, breadsticks, or toast throughout the day.
&gt; Eat foods that are easy on your stomach: white toast, plain yogurt, and clear broth.
&gt; Avoid strong food and drink smells.
&gt; Avoid eating in a room that has cooking odors or is overly warm; keep the living space comfortable but well ventilated.
&gt; Sit up or recline with your head raised for 1 hour after eating.
&gt; Rinse your mouth before and after eating.
&gt; Suck on hard candies such as peppermints or lemon drops if your mouth has a bad taste.
&gt; Eat five or six small meals each day instead of three large meals.
&gt; Do not skip meals and snacks; for many people, having an empty stomach makes nausea worse.
&gt; Choose foods that appeal to you. Do not force yourself to eat any food that makes you feel sick. Do not eat your favorite foods, to avoid linking them to being sick.
&gt; Have liquids throughout the day and drink slowly.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>&gt; Sip only small amounts of liquids during meals because many people feel full or bloated if they eat and drink at the same time.
&gt; Have foods that are neither too hot nor too cold.
&gt; Eat dry toast or crackers before getting out of bed if you have nausea in the morning.
&gt; Plan the best times for you to eat and drink.
&gt; Relax before each cancer treatment.
&gt; Wear clothes that are loose and comfortable.
&gt; Keep a record of when you feel nausea and why.
&gt; Talk with your doctor about the use of anti-nausea medications.</p>
<ul>
<li>Sore Mouth
&gt; Choose foods that are easy to chew, i.e., soft foods such as milkshakes, scrambled eggs, and custards.
&gt; Cook foods until they are soft and tender.
&gt; Cut food into small pieces and use a blender or food processor to puree foods.
&gt; Drink with a straw to help push the drinks beyond the painful parts of your mouth.
&gt; Use a very small spoon to help you take smaller bites, which may be easier to chew.
&gt; Eat cold or room-temperature foods to avoid hurting your mouth with food that is too hot.
&gt; Suck on ice chips to help numb and soothe your mouth.</li>
</ul>
<p>Avoid certain foods and drinks when your mouth is sore, such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>Citrus foods.</li>
<li>Spicy foods.</li>
<li>Tomatoes and ketchup.</li>
<li>Salty foods.</li>
<li>Raw vegetables.</li>
<li>Sharp, crunchy foods.</li>
<li>Drinks that contain alcohol.</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; Do not use tobacco products.
&gt; Visit a dentist at least 2 weeks before starting biological therapy, chemotherapy, or radiation therapy to the head or neck.
&gt; Rinse your mouth 3 to 4 times a day. Mix ¼ teaspoon baking soda, ⅛ teaspoon salt, and 1 cup warm water for a mouth rinse.
&gt; Check your mouth each day for sores, white patches, or puffy and red areas.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Avoid items that can hurt or burn your mouth, such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Mouthwash containing alcohol.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Toothpicks or other sharp objects.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Tobacco products.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Alcohol.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Sore throat and trouble swallowing</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Eat five or six small meals each day instead of three large meals.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Choose foods that are easy to swallow, e.g., milkshakes, scrambled eggs, and cooked cereal.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Choose foods and drinks that are high in protein and calories.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cook foods until they are soft and tender.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cut food into small pieces and use a blender or food processor to puree foods.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Moisten and soften foods with gravy, sauces, broth, or yogurt.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Sip drinks through a straw to make them easier to swallow.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Do not eat or drink things that can burn or scrape your throat, such as:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Hot foods and drinks.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Spicy foods.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Foods and juices that are high in acid.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Sharp or crunchy foods.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Drinks that contain alcohol.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Sit upright and bend your head slightly forward when eating or drinking, and stay upright for at least 30 minutes after eating.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Do not use tobacco products.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Consider tube feedings if your inability to eat is severely affecting your strength.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Taste changes</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Use plastic utensils, and do not drink directly from metal containers if foods taste metallic.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Substitute poultry, fish, eggs, and cheese for red meat.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Consult a vegetarian or cookbook for useful nonmeat, high-protein recipes.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Add spices and sauces to foods; marinate foods.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Eat meat with something sweet, such as cranberry sauce, jelly, or applesauce.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Try tart foods and drinks.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Try to eat your favorite foods, if you are not nauseated. Try new foods when feeling your best.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If tastes are dull but not unpleasant, chew food longer to allow more contact with taste receptors.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>&gt; If smells are an issue, keep foods covered, use cups with lids, drink through a straw, and use a kitchen fan when cooking, or cook outdoors.
&gt; Use sugar-free lemon drops, gum, or mints when experiencing a metallic or bitter taste in the mouth. Use special mouthwashes.
&gt; Visit your dentist and maintain good oral hygiene.</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Vomiting
